A RADICAL REFORM.
“Oh, I feel so Mondayish 1” exclaims the housemother
at her breakfast table on the Monday morning - . Although
the above absurd adjective is not to be found in the dic-
tionary, yet every woman well knows its meaning when
she looks forward to the teeming, steaming, boiling, toil-
ing, temper-trying, hateful washing day. The house is
thrown out of joint, other weekly duties are, as far as
possible, set aside, the-children kept out of the way, a cold
dinner for the family, and a warm reception for anyone
Who intrudes to waste time and to prolong the agony.
Happy, indeed, is the home where “washing day” is a
thing of the past. Every busy, hard-pressed mother
knows the wretchedness and gloom which the present
washing day brings down upon the household. In the
early morning all is prepared for the all important washer-
woman, timed to arrive for an early breakfast, and then
io attack the week or fortnight's batch of soiled linen.
The good-natured husband readily excuses a hastily-pre-
pared breakfast, for he hopes it may betoken the speedy
ending of the dreadful day. The children are packed oif
to school, and then begins in earnest the strife between
the housewife, anxious to get ! everythiug finished in good
time, and the professional laundress, equally anxious to
make the work last over two days. With the greatest
impartiality the laundress divides her attention between
the discussion of light refreshments and the very latest
gossip, the intervals of time being filled up with a gentle
application to her special functions
- Very many of our valued customers to whom wo have
supplied labor-saving appliances in the past, do their own
washing. In every family, in our climate, frequent
changes of underclothing are necessary. Then there are
tlie house linen, the cotton dresses, white coals, and
outer garments ; and all this is heaped up for the poor
house-mother to cleanse, perhaps with the assistance of a
daughter, if there is one old enough. It is not that the
nusband, who also works hard, is unkind. Expenses are
many, economy is imperative, days, of depression and of
trouble must be provided against. So the washing is to
be done, and the wife must do it; and after the imperfect
rest of a sultry night, as she gets ready the breakfast Upon
a hot Monday morning, she feels for a while that her lot
is cheerless, that the apportionment of toil is beyond her
share and beyond her strength ; that somehow a change
should be made. No wonder that fair young girls, after a
few years of married life, become woru-lookmg, and that
they age faster than they ought. The husband is willing
enough to cherish the good wife’s comeliness, and to make
her lot easy, but how ? She does not complain, only says
she feels always “ tired ”
Now, as purveyors of household implements, we nave
long seen our opportunity to enlarge our business by
introducing a thoroughly efficient Washing and Mangling
Machine. Both these services must be combined in the
one article. This we have most perfectly and assuredly
accomplished. The demon of the washing day is not
scotched but killed
We resolved to introduce nothing until absolutely certain
by observation, experiment, and test, that the implement
would be a success. There are hosts of Washing Machines.
The vast majority are childish fads, are injurious, or are
useless. We say and we prove that the Beale Patent
Washer will save in labour of washing in proportion as our
Sewing Machines do in sewing. And in a higher degree,
fer the washing by hands is so much the more laborious.
The Beale Patent Washer may be described as follows :
The vertical tub is very carefully made, with fluted sides.
It is stationary, only the water and the clothes being
agitated by an accurately shaped “dolly,” which is worked
by a fly-wheel. The clothes arc simply dropped in, no
arrangement or packing being wanted. The special
reversing gear moves this dolly swiftly backwards and for-
wards, and a very small expenditure of strength suffices
to lash the water' into a mass of foaming suds. In the
process the clothes are floated to and fro, up and down ;
currents and cross-currents of water are formed, which
meet, and are forced through and through the fabric,
carrying away every particle of dirt and stain. The linen,
being merely suspended in the water, is not rubbed and
lerubbed as in hand-washing. Neither , has anything to
submit to the hand-wringing process, which insensibly
rips, tears, and wears the clothes. Thus the most delicate
fabrics cannot be torn by our system of washing. Every-
one has noticed that their clothes seem to ho as much
worn by washing as by use. This is caused by the fierce
rubbing they get upon washing hoards, or by the hands.
With the Beale Patent Washing Machines this friction is
done away with. Therefore, the clothes will last far longer
than by hand-washing, however careful. When the wash-
ing is done, the tub should he wiped out, so that it be
always sweet and clean. There is no false bottom, and
nothing to accumulate greasy sediments.
When you buy a Machine of any kind,
have a good one.

TREATISE ON NERVOUS DISEASES, THEIR CAUSE,
SYMPTOMS, TREATMENT, AND CURE.
r rilK directions contained in this little treatise will be
_ found of immense benefit to the sufferer; and if these
directions are observed during the early stages of Nervous
or Sexual Debility, Mo Medicines ivill he required, as, by
observing the directions given, the patient can Cure Him-
self.
This admirable little treatise is specially written to
show a method of counteracting the evil effects of indis-
cretions in early life—overwork, irregularity in eating or
drinking, &c. It is the result of much study and long
and successful experience in the treatment of diseases,
and contains a fund of useful information to every suf-
ferer from the following complaints, viz.:— Nervous
Debility, Dizziness, Specks before the Eyes, Pains in the
Back, Defective Memory, Loss of Will Power, Loss of
Energy, Spermatorrhoea, Bashfulness, Diseontentedness,
Constipation, Liver and Kidney Derangements, Atrophy,
Impotence, Impure Blood, Foul Taste in Mouth, Palpita-
tion of Heart. Wakefulness, Despondency, Excessive Ner-
vousness, Inability to Marry, Premature Decay, and other
symptoms experienced by nervous sufferers.
All Should Send for this Little Treatise.
If the complaint is of long standing and requires
medicine, Dr. Fletcher will GUARANTEE TO CURE
ANY CASE OF NERVOUS or SEXUAL DEBILITY
with ONE COURSE OP MEDICINES LASTING FIVE
WEEKS, and will send with medicines A WRITTEN
GUARANTEE to cure same or return the monies paid.
Dr. Fletcher’s treatment is both simple and effective,
and within the reach of the poorest. It is unlike any
other, and with it he has cured over 25,000 patients, and
invites patients to investigate his statements by verifying
the testimonials published in his treatise. The treatise
will be forwarded on receipt 'of two stamps to cover
postage.

Western Australia is a much richer colony than is
generally supposed as regards minerals, agriculture and
pastoralism. —Sir Samuel Griffith.
Professor Brown, of Victoria, who made a thorough
examination of the south-western portion of Western
Australia, states that not the other colonies only, but
even the people of Western Australia evidently do not
know how much there is waiting for development. He
saw thousands of acres in contiguous blocks of tine choco-
late lands, light clay loams, and peaty soils. He thinks
the important features of thedistrict are its grand climate
and regular rainfall.
“ History repeats itself,” and as surely as Sydney and
Melbourne'have made lightning-like progress, so will
PERTH,’
the Capital of West Australia.

SATURDAY, JUNE 21, 1890.
Australia and the Pacific.
Uncomfortable relations have been occa-
sioned between Great Britain and France, in
consequence of apparently insoluble difficul-
ties between the French fishermen on the
Newfoundland hanks and Her Majesty’s
subjects in Newfoundland. The two Powers
are equally anxious to come to an accommo-
dation and both are in a difficulty. With re-
gard to the French, a large marine enterprise,
valuable not only in a commercial sense,
but as a nautical nursery for hardv
mariners, is concerned. The rights of the
Frenchare incompatible with thedesiresof the
Newfoundlanders. The latter desire, in fact,
a monopoly of the fishing-banks to which their
province is the nearest territory. The French,
by long-standing treaty, have a title not
merely to share in these fisheries, but ac-
tually to invade, in a pacific sense, the ad-
jacent shores, forage for bait, and cure their
fish. They stretch their treaty-rights pretty
fully. On the other hand, the Newfound-
landers threaten, if the British Government
do not practically break the treaty with
France and drive their fishermen from those
coasts, to hoist the stars and stripes and
solicit admission into the United States Con-
federation. This is scarcely a serious atti-
tude. for the t nited States' diplomatists are
little likely to hanker after a bleak territory
in a detached and assailable position, with a
big quarrel with a couple of the greatest
Powers of Europe for sole dowry.
* * *
A member of the House of Commons, Sir
Georoe Campbell, who has of late evinced
considerable, although misguided, activity in
connection with colonial affairs, has suggested
that the difficulty may be accommodated by
the French ceding all their treaty-rights in
the Newfoundland fisheries, and the British
in recognition withdrawing their objections
to the appropriation of the New Hebrides by
the French. This proposition has set wag-
ging the jaws of every Chauvinistic donkey
in Australia. - The Sydney Daily Telegraph
breaks out into frantic reclamations on the
subject. It declares that under no conceiv-
able circumstances would these colonies
quietly submit to any arrangement Great
Britain might make to allow the New
Hebrides to [ 'ass into foreign possession.
What the alternative to quietly submitting
might be. is not made very plain. There is
a reference indeed to a stimulation of the
separation movement. But the degree in
which separation from Great Britain would
assist Australia in securing the exclusion of
foreign powers from the Pacific is not eluci-
dated. This is a somewhat important omis-
sion, we conceive, and illustrates the incon-
sequence of the ideas which prevail among
the Australian Chauvinists.
* * •
( There are three positions to be considered.
The first is that by a clamour worked up
in Australia, the Imperial Government is
constrained to alienate the loyalty of the
Newfoundlanders, and risk a quarrel with
the United States, rather than conciliate the
Newfoundlanders, avoid all chance of United
States intrigue with the North American
Colonies—an intrigue which might easily be
made to comprehend the provinces of Canada
peopled by the descendants of the original
French settlers—and get rid of two subjects
of uneasy relations with the French nation.
The second is that the British Government,
refusing to yield to an agitation worked up
in Australia, made the arrangement suggested
by Sir George Campbell, with the result
that Australia, after bitter grumbling, sub-
mitted. The third is that in consequence of
Great Britain taking the last-mentioned
course, Australia determined upon separa-
tion. In the first case, the Imperial authori-
ties having made serious sacrifices, assumed
heavy responsibilities, and hazarded fearful
risks in compliance with Australian demands*
would assuredly have established a claim
upon Australia for corresponding sacrifices
wherever they may be necessary to the wel-
fare of the empire. Should France or the
United States, even although apparently
giving way on this particular point*
nurse a rancorous resentment and find a Vent
for it on some quite different subject, and
with regard possibly to some other region of
the earth, Great Britain would be justified
in representing to Australia : “ This is your
doing ; you must bear your full share of this
trouble.” Under such circumstances the
colonies could not justly complain were the
Australian Squadron withdrawn to operate
on the coast of Madagascar, against the
French, or were British recruiting parties to
parade through Australian towns, enlisting
men for regiments to join the Royal troops
at Guiana or the West Indies. In the
second case Australia would have gained
nothing save a grudge against the mother
country. In the third case, numerous con-
siderations complicate the problem. To
begin with, Great Britain might refuse to
permit Australia to secede from the nation.
If so, all that the Chauvinists would have
accomplished would be the alternatives of a
sullen subjection, or a war of independence
with Great Britain. Let ns Suppose that
such a war resulted in the defeat of the
mother country and the achievement of
Australian independence. How much nearer,
m that event, would we be to the accom*
plishment of the object originally sought,
namely, the exclusion of all foreign
powers from the islands of the Pacific ?
It !s an axiom in historical Science that a
nation emerging from a successful war is
only a little less exhausted than a nation
emerging from an unsuccessful one. Aus-
tralia, drawing a long breath after so des-
perate a struggle as that with Great Britain,
would be in a pretty condition to commence
the job of despatching expeditions to drive
the French from New Caledonia, the Ger-
mans from New-Guinea, the Solomons, New
Britain, and New Ireland. Fach of these
places is nearer to some portion of the coast
of Australia than are (lie New Hebrides I
Pursuing the matter to its ridiculous ex-
tremity, once the Germans (and the British)
were driven out of New Guinea, ami theiV
portion of that island made part of the Aus-
tralian territory, the same ride of policy
which prompted the whole series of events
would require an immediate attack upon the
Dutch, whose part of the Island would then
actually touch Australian possessions all
along a frontier of a thousand miles or so a
frontier constituted by an imaginary line
drawn on a map.
# ' * *
Next, supposing the Dutch %o 'share the
unhappy fete of Great Britain, Germany,
and I ranee, and to be driven headlong out
of their possessions by the overwhelming
might of Austmlian armies ami thfc
task would he merely commenced. Australia
would, by series of feats unpaialleled in the
history of successful war fete, have huV>ed
three great nations, or suc-
cessively, out of those possessions which lay
so close to Australia as to ekeite jealousy or
apprehension. New Guinea would be ours ;
New r Caledonia, the New r Hebrides, the
Solomons, New Britain, New' Ireland would
be subject to the flag of the Southern Cross.
Surely that should content us. But no! the
miserable policy of keeping all " foreigners ”
at a distance from Australian territory would
like the villain in the melodramas—still
pursue us. By these extensions of our
possessions we should, instead of removing
the evil which stmig us into an activity so
remarkable, have simply aggravated it. We
could never feel secure in the New Hebrides
while Great Britain held Fiji, the distance
between the tw r o island groups being less
than half that which separates the New
Hebrides from the Australian continent.
Nor could our nervous minds be at ease with
regard to our New Guinea territory while
the Moluccas were in foreign hands.
* # #
In tine, the whole theory of the cranks
who insist on every island or group in the
Pacific which lies within a week’s steaming
of Australia being kept out of the hands of
foreigners, leads to a ludicrous and gro-
tesque i ’'duetto ad absnrdum. Once admit its
validity and there is no escape from the con-
clusion that Australian liberties will never be
safe until the entire globe has been brought
into subjection in the interests of Austra-
lians. lo sally outside the natural boun-
daiies of the continent is not the way to keep
possible foes at a distance. On the contrary,
it is the certain plan to turn friends into
foes and to thrust ourselves within teach of
these blows while weaKVJurig ourselves by
elongation. • x
* * '•. #
The true policy of Young Austin lift is the
policy of concentration ; of abstention fpom
meddling with territories beyond her own
obvious geographical limits. It is surprising
that With so recent, so striking, and so suc-
cessful an example as that which the United
States have furnished in this identical par-
ticular, there can be. found any persons so
crass as to counsel ft policy of external
aggression for Australia. What is advocated
is in effect as though the early American
statesmen had counselled their people to
insist upon the evacuation of the West India
Islands by all the European powers which
owned them. To-day, thanks to the wisdom
which has taught the Republic to refrain
from intermeddling with affairs exterior to
its own territory, the United States has
grown into a power which is potent to absorb
the West Indies at any time it Play think
the game worth the candle. Ret Australians
take heed to the lesSOriS Of history, and turn
the ear of derision to those hysterical coun-
sellors who would fain prompt them to ft
display of imbecility.

Moreover, the scheme we advocate has other
moral Apparently the existent
system is tolerated because it is felt tthat mankind
will gamble to tha bitter end ; bull if the evil pas-
sion is directed into a new channel ft good Inany
of its worst features toay be got rid of. The State
will then have ft direct interest in the abolition of

ill I’efcurh for ft reft'-onable fee, he-is prepared to

finds ft rieW fthd more lawful outlet. Unless it can
be demonstrated that all these and many more of
their kind are reputable and valuable citizens, we
claim that the change We suggest is conceived in
the best interests of public morality-.
% # *
We are Well aware that a shriek of anger and
derision from the Church at large will greet these
proposals, but while the Church continues to live
so largely and directly on lotteries and raffles and
other sinful devices, not to mention the gifts of
the gamblers who are among its chief sup-
porters, it has little reason to complain if the State
phooses lb do the same. Moreover, the Church
has devised nothing since the days of the Crusades
which promised in the least degree to supply that
craving for excitement which is rooted in the
human soul, and that old diversion proved so un-
satisfactory and unprofitable that it has long since
Ceased to be regarded as ft rational amusement.
The WOrld has tacitly resolved that a life of
hftmble, plain respectability is too maddening in
its colourless monotony to be endured, and it is
always suffering from a desire to go out and do
something to prevent the moss growing on its
weary, torpid soul. It Used to sally forth and kill
somebody in times Of mental exhaustion, but this
light entertainment has lost inUch Of its popularity,
and is rioW, Regarded ft's a business instead
Of ift PebVeation. It grew excited over vvild-
heast fights, and public floggings and hangings,
and refreshed itself by watching men pound each
other to death, but all these things have fallen off
more or less, It has been looking for and finding
some form of diversion—generally a sinful one—
ever since it Was first furnished with intelligence,
but gambling has outlasted all the rest. Respec-
tability is ft solemn, portentous thing, and as it
has consistently opposed itself to amusement the
bulk of humanity refuses to be respectable. It
wants to be amused, and a tall, black hat and a
'funeral are not thrilling enough for the purpose.
And, therefore, despite the protests ceaselessly
issued by the Spectre bf Respectability, the larger
paid Of the human race keeps on gambling as the
readiest means of escape from its own weariness,
fthd as the evil seems to defy all remedy, We
Suggest, that -it should be made, if possible, a
profitable and agreeable sin, instead of being a
profane and riotous offence and a dead loss as well.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
The Bankers Repentance.
o


Holy Roman ruffianism, but as nobody seems to
want to besiege Londonderry any more, the nar-
rative might as well be laid away in company with
the Siege of Nicopolis and the other fluff and
debris of history; and besides, the besiegers and
besieged are all"dead together, and the Orangeman
of to-day has no more connection with that episode
than he has with the capture of Bagdad. The horrors
of ’9B will, of course, be exposed once more to the
public gaze, and if anybody can be found living at
the present day who had anything to do with the
horrors of ’9B, we would suggest that the Unionist
party should promptly hang that person, and then
change the subject, and go on to talk of something
fresh. The dead are an old, used-rrp topic ; and,
moreover, man is short and history is very long,
and there is barely time enough to look after the
living. And, so far as the living is concerned,
the case is this : The world has finally recognised
that one man cannot justly belong to another man,
and it is equally logical to assume that one nation
cannot lawfully belong to another. The right of
every man to own himself implies the right of a
nation to do the same. As the Irish representa-
tives in the Imperial Parliament are invariably
voted down by the numerical superiority of the
English members, and Ireland, in consequence, is
governed by England, the Irish nation does not
own itself—it is at present foreign property.
Whether Ireland has benefited by English rule
has nothing to do with the case ; whether it would
go to the devil under Irish rule is a purely ex-
traneous question. The agnostic or the idolater
may be going headlong to perdition, but public
opinion has reluctantly recognised the fact that if
he insists on it he must be allowed to go, and that
the Christian has no right to put him in gaol or use
other violent means to save his soul if he doesn’t
want it saved. The inalienable right of every
citizen to go to hell if he is really howling
for a place there, is admitted by the code
of every European nation; and even if Mr.
Abigail’s Orange delegation should prove in every
town and village of Australia, from Sydney to
Perth, and from Adelaide to the remotest regions
beyond the alligator-line, that Ireland —if allowed
to run her own Government without the benefi-
cent interference of England—is going to Gehenna
straight, we can only say briefly that it is her
blessed privilege to go if she feels that way.
What the delegation really wants to prove is, that
it is right and lawful for one man to own another
man without his consent, and for one aggregation
of men to own another when the second lot want
to own themselves; and if the Orange representa-
tives can’t demonstrate this fact then they are
fooling round for no visible purpose. And as it is
not worth anybody’s while to come 16,000 miles
merely to offer a few weary platitudes about some-
body who isn’t alive any longer, we trust that
delegation will either come armed with a few
fresh observations or else will think better of it
and stay at home.
That Laughter in Court.
An incident at which the cynical outside world
sniggered bitterly, while the allegorical figure of
Justice probably blushed, marked the conclusion of
Counsel Purves’ indictment of the Premier Perma-
nent Building Society crowd. Rather, it should be
said that the outside world —which is of necessity
excluded from ‘Melbourne City Court just now-
sniggered as aforesaid when it got the Herald’s
report. After having dealt for two whole days
with a series of alleged financial juggles, pasting
together of ledger leaves, replacements of cash
abstractions with an official’s 1.0.U.’s and
business arrangements of a peculiarly “ fishy ”-
looking character—after having, in fact, opened
what can fairly be described as a strong prima-
facie case against some of the influential
defendants, Purves ventured to suggest that he
would at least succeed in obtaining their committal
to a higher court. Not a very extravagant pro-
position, as police-court cases go, nor one which is
usually received with other than calm indifference
by counsel for the accused party. For some
reason, however, the mere mention of a prima facie
case in connection with an ex-member of Parlia-
ment, two ex-Cabinet Ministers still in the House,
a “blood-relation” of an almighty paper, and a son
of a leading politician, threw their legal representa-
tives into a mild state of merriment. But when
Purves went on to hint at various degrees of
criminality calling for various degrees of punish-
ment —Great Justice! how his learned friends
roared, to bo sure. Even the defendants smiled
at that. Punishment! It was too droll. Punish-
ment for these gentlemen, some of whom were not,
at that time, particular about being present or
absent from the court where they were nominal
prisoners !
The counsel laughed with the lawyers,
Each lawyer grinned at a beak—
What, punish these here top-sawyers !
Well, haven’t some chaps got cheek !
Egad, it was quite too funny,
A doosidly comical stroke.
(Investors who’ve lost their money—
They haven’t yet seen the joke.)
Time will show whether this significant hilarity
was inspired by secret knowledge or by shrewd
suspicion merely. Perhaps, indeed, it was the
spontaneous outcome of a simple faith which the
riotous barristers have orr the complete innocence
of their clients severally and collectively. Any-
way, such a combined legal effort to laugh down a
serious charge was disgraceful, and will set decent
people speculating on the possible existence of a
fraudulent conspiracy of influence outside Mel-
bourne City Court, a conspiracy for further estab-
lishing the fact that there may be one law for the
Rich and another for the Poor.

Awake on His Beat.
There were several reasons why, during the course
of the recent strike of dock-labourers in London,
the strikers were not vigorously smashed and per-
secuted. One was, no doubt, because there had
been roused a certain degree of shocked feeling
among the Philistine classes, in consequence of
having the appalling condition of those workers
forced upon their attention. Rank and Wealth
were somewhat divided on this particular subject.
The great Landed Interest which includes the
Peerage, did not feel itself specially concerned
even though the dock-labourers should wrench an
extra penny per hour from the griping hands of
commercial Capital, It was not unfashionable—
not mnuvais ton— to sympathise in a qualified sort
of way with tire strikers. The situation was con-
siderably different fromthat of the Trafalgar Square
sympathy-with-lreland meetings. In that instance
there was complete solidarity among the classes,
with the full weight of the’Government at their
backs. Consequently the police were turned loose
upon the people, and broke brave John Burns’
head amidst universal applause and considerable
enthusiasm. The vigour of the metropolitan
Police on that occasion was highly lauded by the
Classes and their newspaper organs. Cultured
peers and lusty squires rubbed their hands and
chuckled to thj.uk Irow fully tire Force could be
depended upoTto smash any assemblage of the
People who dared to collect in protest against any
wrong, however grievous, and rrnder any circum-
stances, however legal. They reckoned, no doubt,
that on that occasiou their hounds, the Police, were
“ blooded,” and thenceforth could _ always be
reckoned upon to worry those vermin, the mob.
Subsequent events, however, indicate that the real
result of that collision between the policemen and
the People has been to set the blue-coated People
reflecting with respect to tbeir actual relation to
the People in fustian. When the dock-labourers
assembled in thousands, another reason, probably
more potent than the one we first mentioned, why
they were not charged by the Police and clubbed
till they dispersed, was that “ the Police could not
be depended upon.” Now comes the signifi-
cant intelligence that the Policemen them-
selves are forming a Union. The next pro-
bable step will be that the Policeman’s
Union has affiliated with the British Trades-
Union. The Postmen, too, we are rejoiced to
observe, are in motion with a similar tendency, so
that when next a body of special constables to club
the mob is called for, it seems unlikely that Saint
Martin’s le Grand will furnish its usual contingent.
In short, the official people in the civil ranks have
begun to appreciate the fact that the mob consists
simply of their own class out of uniform. The
next body of the British people to learn a lesson
so contagious will assuredly be the soldiery, and
once these shall have thoroughly appreciated the
truth, the process of crushing social agitation with
batons, and smashing it with bullets, bayonets,
sabres and grape-shot will be unavailable to the
oligarchs of Great Britain. After that —the
Deluge.
Mr. Kidd, M.L.A., is understood to complain
that the country papers as a rule, report the put-
pourings of himself and his fellow members in a
way that makes them utterly incapable of recogni-
tion. We cordially endorse the statement, and
only object to it as being too weak. The Parlia-
mentary reports of the country press are demora-
lising and contrary to the noblest instincts of
humanity, arrd also they come as near to lying as
any printed matter well can. Listen to this for
instance :
Mr. Gamp, in an eloquent speech, denounced the mea-
sure as unstatesmarilike and deficient in scope. He then
briefly sketched the outlines of a comprehensive scheme
which he proposed to substitute for the inefficient pro-
posals of the Ministry. His attack was probably the most
damaging the Cabinet has met with this session, and it
will not soon recover from the blow.
Compare the meagre, bald, naked wretchedness of
this colorless picture with the burning eloquence
of the orator as it ought to appear in the country
“ horgan,” and see what these few lines of skimpy
drivel amount to by comparison :
Mr. Gamp : “Mr. Speaker, the Bill now afore the ’Ouse
is hall rot an’ oughter be throwed through the winder. It
haint no good to nobody. (A voice : ‘ Rats ! ’) The per-
son as said rats is a horphan blaokbeetle dyin’ in a soap-
box. Why didn’t somebody throw him away when he
was young ? As I was sayin’ the Bill ain’t no good. The
conshtitooency I reppersent wants to be purvided with a
noo post-hoffis, an’ the Guv’ment ain’t dredged the creek,
and in consekens of this neglect trade is a failin’ hoff.
(Derisive cheers.) The Iron, member that interrupted
me jest now was found on a dsorstep. (Cries of ‘ Come
outside.’) I won’t. You’re a broken-down bandicoot.
(A voice, “ You’re a liar.”) I ain’t! Your another ! So’s
yer mother ! I’ll fight yer for tuppence. The institoo-
shuns of this country her gone to the dogs an’ I’m pre-
pared to camp here till I take root afore this ’ere Bill gets
through. There oughter he a loan floated for £10,000,000
an’ railways made everywhere. I ain’t going to be choked
hoff. I moves as a hamendment that the Gov’ment be
called upon to make lines all hover the country an’ that
taxation be abolished right hoff. (Loud applause.) An’
I wants tfiis ’ere done hinstantly.”

In conversation with a Pall Mall Gazette
interviewer, Francis Abigail has let his tongue
wag freely on “the sentimental attachment of
Australia to the mother country. ” After stating
that “ if a poll of the Australian people could be
taken on the subject to-morrow, there would be an
overwhelming majority in favor of maintaining our
allegiance to the mother land,” he goes on to allege
that at the notorious “Queen’s Fund” (Jubilee)
meeting at Sydney Exhibition building—“not a
ticket-meeting, mind you, the hall was cram-
med ; 20,000 people were present; and when some
disaffected fellow shouted out a word of abuse at
the mention of the-Queen, he was pitched out of the
window , and the meeting was enthusiastic in its
loyalty. That is a type of the feeling of Sydney.”
Splendid lie! Everyone in Sydney knows that
the spaces round the platform were filled by the
back-door reception of Orangemen, who helped to
pack the great hall ere the general public were
admitted, that a number of prize-fighters and
ball-kickers were specially engaged, that sailors
from the men-of-war in harbour attended in the
Imperial interest, that the Permanent Force was
in attendance with loaded guns to stifle, even by
bloodshed, all noisy demonstrations of disloyalty,
and that the Governor was escorted to the hall by a
pack of professional bruisers. What happened ?
Why, in spite of the packing of the meeting, in
spite of all the previous “ chucking-out” of
openly declared Republicans by Orangemen,
and boxing and kicking bullies, yet when
the matter was put to the vote there was no
visible preponderance on the side of “royalty”;
the votes of those surrounding the platform went
for Imperialism, while the hands of those separated
from it by a drilled body of privately-admitted
person’s went up like a forest for Republicanism
and Independence. Furthermore, incensed by ill-
usage, by the “ Kentish fire ” of Orangemen, by
being refused the right to move amendments and
of speech through their representatives, the
penned-off Australian Nationalists then made a
rush for the platform and, but for the opportune
appearance of Lady Carington, some damage
would have been done to ultra-loyalists and the
professional bullies, their hired assistants. In a
squarely-conducted and truly public mass meeting
Imperialism would be licked in Sydney by three
votes to two. And well do the Royalists know it.
Dear Bulletin, —As an axiom of sound com-
mercial morality, there is more risk than reve-
nue to be derived from treating the vagaries
of the cable liar on the basis of abstract truth ;
but if he be not actually on the solid rock
in his statement that the police of London
have formed themselves into a union, he is
only trading on the future with a fair margin of
discount. And when that union is formed and
takes its place among the Trade and Labour
organisations of the old world, another link that
binds the workers to the festering rottenness of
Monarchy will be suddenly snapped. Despite their
uniforms, their discipline, their allegiance to
the tyranny imposed by system and upheld by
custom, the sympathies of the police were with
their suffering fellow men, the dock labourers,
throughout the recent strike. The authorities
knew that and provided for emergencies by con-
stant watchfulness, and by scattered detachments
of troops throughout the metropolis, armed and
ready for the contingency which they had but too
good reasons for dreading. There was a strong
bond of human sympathy between the police and
the men on strike, each side knew it and both sides
were bound by it. The police were the anti-friction
medium between the Purple and the People, the
buffer between the civil and the nrilitary elements
of strife. Probably before another stage is reached
in the great social upheaval which is looming closer
and more distinctly day by day, the medium will
have been absorbed into the ranks of unionism,
the buffer have been withdrawn and enveloped in
the rapidly extending halo of righteous feeling for
the starving pauper and the gaunt victims of greed
and oppression. And then? Well, before the
final bitter lesson has been learnt which will drive
Tommy Atkins off on the same track, we shall
perhaps hear that a detachment of the household
troops has been ordered to an East End slum
rrnder the command of some pompous idiot —whose
father was first a pimp and then a lord with a
capital L —a youth of simian aspect and imperfect
speech, whose sole duty, after the Riot Act has
been read, will consist in giving the orders to
“ Make weady,” “Pwesent,” “Ah, fiah ! doncher-
know.” That “ Eiah!” will sound the knell of the
monarchy. —W.

A few months ago Melbourne Age dropped upon
the little Herald for reproducing the former’s
cablegrams as news from Our Own (Herald)
Correspondent. Now the Herald is taking a
legitimate revenge by furnishing unpleasantly full
reports of the Premier Building Association case,
calling particular attention to the Gourlay-Syme
relationship—which we do not say has anything to
do with the case—and generally doing its level best
to counteract the Age’s sustained efforts to throw
ridicule upon the case for the prosecution. Those
who can read between the lines of daily journalism
find much to interest them in the attitudes that
the various Melbourne organs are assuming with
regard to this cause celebre. The Herald points
out how the defendants begin to show symptoms
of “intense anxiety.” The Argus is evidently a
prey to conflicting emotions. On the one hand it
is most undesirable, you know, to have charges
against persons of “position,” and, on the other-
hand, it would be nice to take a rise out of the
nasty penny “largest circulation.” The Age de-
fiantly flourishes what looks like a brief for the de-
fendants in the face of the world. The Standard,
being in a roundabout way a sort of thirty-second
cousin of the Age, publishes colourless accounts
of the trial. And the Telegraph, saintly organ
of professional religion, teetotalism, and genteel
land-booming, sheds a feeble ray of dull respecta-
bility over the charges, seeming to hint that cri-
minal conduct under a tall hat is not to be thought
of. But as the public like to get the latest,
fullest, and spiciest information for after-dinner
consumption, the Herald is probably forming a
larger amount of honest opinion than all the other-
papers put together.
The smart tactics of the Victorian Opposition, in
declining to discuss Federation until there is
something tangible to talk about, have bustled
little Gillies considerably. The three weeks, at
least, he had reckoned upon getting for the com-
pletion of his Railway Bill were suddenly reduced
to as marry days, and the utter impossibility of
dealing out 1000 miles of rail at such short notice
on terms calculated to make the Government
“ right ” in dangerous quarters, caused D. Gillies,
Premier, to put in a very unquiet Sabbath after-
noon. By this time, probably, the Bill, with
all its imperfections on its wicked head, is
before a disgusted House. The land-syndicators
who find their tracts of paper-purchased country
neglected, the smaller speculators in bits of
suburban property which they thought were to
be wanted for- the new line, you know, who are
now told that “ it can’t, be considered at present,
dear boy,” arrd, in fact,' three-fourths of the
glorious army of jobbers are gnashing their old
gums this moment. It’s very hard on little Dun-
can. If the exchequer- were good for 10,000
miles of new railways he might slap ’em
down in the proper places and flood the
country with rolling-stock. But the colony
happens to be a “broker,” and the trouble of
ruling by the favour- of a crowd of hyenas, who are
likewise “ brokers,” is great indeed. If they had
all taken up a lot of Tramway Cos. shares, they
might be able to hold on for a bit longer. Now
they are desperate men, who may at any minute
stand upon the floor of the House and belch forth
appalling statements under the influence of strong
drink, while the small minority, made “right ” by
the Bill, vainly endeavour to drown the dreadful
revelations in shouts of “ Shame !”
David Gaunson, so long held up to scorn by the
Age, is at present in high favour with that “ demo-
cratic ” organ. His attack on Purves last week,
made with the apparent object of getting himself
ejected from Melbourne City Court, was graciously
received by the Age “as the certain outcome of
the appearance of Mr. Gaunson and Mr.
Purves on different sides ” —rowdy David’s
side happening to be that of the Pre-
mier B.S. defendants. “Mr. Gaunson Protests ”
was the Age heading, leading the guileless
reader to suppose that he had got something im-
portant to protest against, instead of being en-
gaged in kicking up a senseless shindy. Did any
readers, The Bulletin wonders, recall to mind the
day of yore when Gaunson’s “ protests” in Par-
liament were reckoned up as “disgraceful scenes,”
and when his statements were described as un-
worthy of notice by the newspapers which now
smile upon him? Faugh!

A largo number of Australian and N.Z. paper ß
are just now discussing the question whether Mr.
Joseph, proprietor of the Tam worth News, should
not have refused to give up the name of his own
contributor, Martin, now being criminally pro-
secuted for libel by Commissioner Eddy. Says a
Darling Downs journal:—
Mr. Joseph swears he had the writer’s permission, to do
so, but even supposing the permission were given with a
full knowledge of the consequences, the action is no
honour to journalism. Mr. Eddy is manifestly anxious to
get at the fountainhead of the libel, so-called, and the
anxiety is more natural than philosophical. We hold
that the onus of guilt, if guilt there be, in such oases
lies upon him who gives the libel vitality. Martin
or anyone else might devote his days and nights
to • concocting charges without anybody being the
worse or wiser unless a means were found of giving the
charges currency. Nobody controlling a newspaper is
under any obligation to publish anybody’s communica-
tion. If he does, it is of his own free will and election,
always supposing the man is worth his salt, and from that
moment he assumes the whole responsibility, and should
see it out like a man. Many a good man under such cir-
cumstances makes a grievous mistake, but the mis-
take is his own ; and many a good man makes
no mistake in equity, although a huge blunder in
law. In such cases the public should come to
his rescue. Under no circumstances should he attempt
to shift his responsibility to other shoulders, and
whoever would wring from another a reluctant consent to
assume that responsibility does not possess courage up to
the height of his position, while no epithet is too base for
him who would betray confidence by giving up the name
of a writer. The Press is pretty well educated rip to these
ethics, the iguorants not being many, but the public, we
grieve to sec, even men holding such positions as Mr.
Eddy and Judge Cooi-kr, retain an invincible desire to go
for the writer.
Which all bears on the discussion about signed
articles raised by Salomons, Q.C.
What is Cruelty ?
The Divorce Bill now being discussed by the
Legislative Council of New South Wales pro-
vides that the courts may dissolve marriage, not
only for adultery, but for habitual drunkenness,
neglect to provide, desertion, habitual cruelty,
and other causes. While much controversy has
been indulged in as to whether divorce can be
granted by the State for any cause whatever,
little attention has been paid to the clauses of the
Bill which specify the grounds on which it is pro-
posed to legalise divorce. This is a blunder on
the part of those who oppose divorce. It is plain
that the Bill will pass. Those who have the
measure in hand should see that their intentions
are clearly expressed in the various clauses, while
those who oppose it might usefully employ them-
selves in endeavouring to make the best of what is
inevitable.
# * *
There can, of course, be very little dispute as to
the interpretation which the Bill will bear, so far
as most of its clauses are concerned. Adultery
does not require an act of Parliament for its defini-
tion ; desertion is defined as being for a period of
three years or more; neglect to provide is self-
explanatory ; drunkenness is rather too common in
this community to admit of much mistake in its
recognition. Not so cruelty. There are some
forms of cruelty which no one is likely to mistake.
No jury or judge is likely to regard the trouncing
of a woman with a prop-stick as wholesome
fun, nor hauling her round by the hair
of the head as mistaken fondness. There are
other forms of playfulness which are sometimes
indulged in that may be viewed differently. There
is cruelty mental, cruelty moral, and cruelty
physical; we might perhaps add also cruelty to
the feelings. The question does not, however,
admit of being summed up in this fashion. What
is cruelty to a pretty woman might be merely a
charitable attention if applied to a plain one.
When we were in America last year a book was
issued at Washington on the subject of Divorce
and Marriage Laws. It was prepared under a
special Act of Congress, and contains much in-
teresting information. We made at the time some
extracts giving the facts which were held by some
of the State Courts to sustain the charge of cruelty.
The selections were made not because the items
were more serious than the others or more ridi-
culous, but simply because they were short and
pithy. The following are complaints made by
women for which divorces were granted
Plaintiff alleges that defendant does not wash himself,
thereby inflicting on plaintiff great mental anguish.
Defendant would not permit plaintiff to walk on the
streets with her relations ; he spoke disrespectfully of her,
calling her a “ rip.”
Plaintiff says she is subject to sick headaches, that grow
worse when she smells tobacco. Defendant uses tobacco,
and thus aggravates her headaches.
Defendant was guilty of cruelty in not providing a sup-
ply of water at his house, and in not repairing the said
house so as to make it comfortable.
Defendant would not support plaintiff, and, when re-
quested to do so, replied that he would not work his “ toe-
nails off for any woman.”
“ Defendant cut off my bangs by force.”
“My husband would never cut his toe-nails, and 1 was
scratched very severely every night, especially as he was
very restless.”
Plaintiff says defendant will not work during the week,
but on Sundays he puts on his old clothes and works hard
all day, which conduct sorely grieves plaintiff.
Another case may be instanced, wherein—
A rich girl disliking her guardian, went to the hospital
with the intention of marrying a dying man, thinking
that as a widow she would lie freer and have more control
over her estate. She married a man seemingly at the
point of death; but the man recovered, and the wife
brought suit for cruelty and fraud.
The complaints of the poor husbands were nut
quite so varied, but the following cases show that
wives were not without a taste for devilish
cruelty
Defendant violently upbraided plaintiff and said to
him : “You are no man at all ’’—thus causing him mental
suffering and anguish.
Plaintiff says : “ My wife refused to keep my clothing in
repair. She even refused to cook and never sewed on my
buttons.” A witness testified that he had seen plaintiff
with only one button on his vest. Plaintiff further
alleged that his wife would not allow him to go to fires at
night ; that if he went she would keep him awake until
three o’clock quarrelling.
Defendant struck plaintiff a violent blow with her
bustle.
Defendant has frequently evinced towards plaintiff a
hasty temper.
During the last year defendant has struck plaintiff with
pokers, flat-irons, and other hard substances.
Defendant aimed a red-hot poker at the head of plaintiff.
We once saw a noted book of Roman Catholic
theology, compiled by some learned person of lorig
ago, for the use of confessors, but we should say very
little used. This book is in Latin, and contains a
catalogue of all the sins that it was possible for man,
woman, or child to have committed at the time of
its publication. We say at the time of publication,
since, in these days of syndicates, tramways, pubhc
parks, &c., there are plenty of sins committed
which could hardly have occurred to the author.
Our suggestion is that an index be prepared, set
ting forth all the acts of cruelty which a man
can inflict on a woman, or a woman inflict
on a man who is her husband. Those act.
which the legislature think are worthy of
divorce might be distinguished by an asterisks
There are men in the Legislature who are capabe!
of drawing up such a list, so far as husbands would
be affected, and there is fair reason for supposing
their wives could compile, if not from actual ex-
perience at least from hearsay, an equally com-
plete list from a woman’s point of view. Such an
index could easily be added to as new modes of
torture unfold themselves, and as civilisation ad-
vances, acts which were not originally distin-
guished by * could be promoted to the full rank of
major offences. Henry Siebel.

846 Macquarie-street North, Sydney, June 18,1890.
My Dear Moorabinda,—
The first vice-regal reception of the season at Sydney on
52th inst. was attended by a large, though not exactly all-
wool, flock—the per-centage of strange, lost-looking people,
who stood about looking the picture of misery in their too
Obviously “ Sunday dresses,” being large and depressing;
tut the day was superb. Lady Carington, ably lieu-
tenanted by her lord, who is apparently gifted with a tire-
less hand-grip (I wasn’t the first arrival, by a long way,
hut the V.R. greeting “bust” a button off my Suedes),
received all the time in the red drawing-room and wore a
gown in shot-pink silk made with a plain front and water-
f ill back over a tournure, the bodice and skirt lined with
i:arrow ribbon velvet in fawn-brown; velvet sleeves in
f awn-brown only slightly ridged at the shoulder, coronet
bonnet of pink roses and velvet with shot velvet strings ;
brown silk parasol. Mayoress Burdekin wore a very hand-
some gown in true prune velvet and'Russell cord, the
hack made perfectly 7 straight over a slight indication of
fulness ; the bodice having velvet sleeves and a Zouave of
hlack braiding, small bonnet en suite ; Miss Walenn, green-
nnd-blue tartan trimmed with velvet, dark-blue hat and
feather ; Miss Irene Vanbrugh, a sombre, limpish attire in
grey tweed snowflaked, cut low in the neck and bound
about the body in a weird way with black silk, Tosca hat
in black felt and feathers, with the indispensable veil, of
course. There were few distinctly choice costumes on
view, the majority clinging to velvet sleeves and a floral
hat with great tenacity 7 . Much loud adulation was
indulged in over the oil-painting, hy a local artist, of the
Misses Alexandra, Marjorie, and Ruperta Carington, in
which Alexandra is depicted perpendicular in a drab
riding-suit, while her two little sisters sit on a garden*
eeat backed by a spreading tree.
Apropos to ladies’ clubs, the best authorities agree that
they are—to put if mildly—not always a success, owing to
feminine jealousy and the absence of those assurers of big
dividends —billiards and a bar. Some energetic club'
women organise exhibitions of old lace, quaint jewellery,
and any oddities they have in hand, to swell the general
fund ; and the afternoon tea, and the letting of the bed-
rooms yields a profit, of course, but they always lose on
the restaurant. And the jealousy comes in over the black-
balling—“ You pepper my friend, I pepper yours,” and so
i he thing dies. An amusing feature of a ladies’ club,
which existed six years ago in Melbourne, was the rush
for seats by the window looking into the street. Even the
most immaculate club-marm likes to stick a stuffed bird
in her hat and strike a thoughtful pose for the benefit ot
the pavement.
On 6th inst. a well-attended gathering of the committee
of the Women’s College fad took place in Sydney Town
Hall when the chief business was the totting up of the
booklets: £B3 16s. showing as the result of 1400 circulars
and the undermining persuasion of the determined col-
lectresses. At present the fund stands at £968 11s. 3d in
hard cash, and to ensure fresh and vigorous recruits it
was resolved that “ the names of ladies who assist in the
collection shall appear in the newspapers ” —also, that
those who give from ten shillings up will have their labels
writ large in the daily press. That is the sort of thing to
incite the professional philanthropist.
Weary of the vagaries of the woman wno runs riot over
draperies with an empty purse one or two long-suffering
firms elsewhere have adopted an original plan for separat-
ing the sheep from the goats. Thus: A man stands at
the door and politely asks each woman whether she wants
to “ price ” or to buy. If a buyer she passes in untaxed,
but if she vaguely declares she only wants to look round
then is she charged sixpence for a ticket entitling her to
feel delicate silks with soiled gloves—stroke rich plush
the wrong way or order bales of calico from the top
story, after the manner of her kind—only she must show
her pasteboard to each victim. It seems a good idea—
but, then, I can imagine—can’t you ?—how ninety out of
every hundred “ prices” would look Cerberus fair in the
ocular with the glance of a w'ell-behaved angel as they
softly admitted they had come to shop.
Vice-regality assembled on 10th inst. at Sydney Cri-
terion to sample “ The Parvenu ” and see Pattie
Browne climb a tree —which is no hardship to a
soubrette with round ankles, by the way. The d.c.
dressing was too de Medici for words. Lady Carington
sported a trained dress in mauve-pink silk with high back
and collar and puffed elbow sleeves (our Social Beacon
never dons decollette at the theatre) with mauve aigrette
and diamonds in the hair; Suede gloves and diamond
bangles with long opera-cloak of cream, accordeon-pleated
chiffon flowered with an exceeding flower, and finished
with a yoke and Medici collar in sap-green velvet. Miss
Walenn, yellowish frock with brown moire sash. Another
lady in the vice-regal party resembled somebody's shadow
inasmuch as she was in raven’s-whig black from head to
heel—gown, gloves, bracelets, and all; and Baron Caring-
ton looked like a barber’s excelsior with his hair parted
in the centre and a white weskit. Pattie Browne
assumes a mocking horror reminiscent of a wild
co-mingling of a red-hot poker with a “ thumb” of blue

In the realistic performance of that bouncing chestnut,
“ After Dark,” at Her Majesty’s, the only anachronism
your little Sappho found to object to was the incompre-
hensible horror of the slavey in the last thrill over the

At the vice-regal reception I was sorry to see that the
new collars—cut open in the front—obtained with the
majority of people, no matter what shape or color their
necks were, and as this collar reveals the whole front of
the throat results weren’t alw'ays artistic. A woman with
a snowflake gullet with no visible sinews in it looks like a
picture with one on ; but a female with a fowl’s claw
cuticle, or a line of sunburn where her linen used to go,
had better avoid it as she would cold poison. What a lot
of muscles there are in the front of the neck, to be sure—
did you ever look in the mirror at your neck after a
Turkish bath ? No linen or lace is worn with the new
collar, which is simply made of the trimming or material
of the gown ; they are generally velvet just now. Fortu-
nately these pretty but impossible collars are not Hob-
son’s choice, and the charitable Medici collar still covers a
multitude of blemishes, even though it does make the
wearer resemble a hooded lizard with his back up.
Quite the latest artistic idea is to get your walls up to
suit the prevailing complexion of the people inside, and I
don’t think the “ reformer” is a bit too early, do you?
Many a tow-haired daughter has just missed a proposal
through the paternal taste leading to a mural mousey-
drab ; or, if her looks were ebon, to a staring
pea-green—but I wonder how people will manage who
have offspring with assorted complexions and misfit
hair ? I suppose in that case they will have their walls
painted in becoming stripes, and then tomato-haired
Sissy can pose effectively backed by a streak of geranium-
red, while dark-haired Mary Ann can languish against a
ribbon of old gold.
The mossy old custom of entering a ball-room hooked
on to a man’s arm is relegated to the limbo of bad form,
and the correct thing just now is for the lady to walk a
little ahead of the gentleman. A man and woman arm-
in-arm is one of the most awful sights possible unless they
are matched in height and can keep in step, but, as a rule,
he is six feet and she is five, and he walks with a preppy
stride while she bobs along in a hasty, undignified way,
clinging tenaciously to the lofty support accorded her
while people grin behind their fans. And how much
worse it is when she Is Goliath and he is David, and he
has to hook his elbow on a level with his shoulder to save
her having to bend double.
If the Sydney vice-regalities continue to whiz as they
have whizzed for the last week or so, their august suc-
cessors will either have to begin tearing round violently
from the moment they touch terra firma or else resign
themselves to the most odious kind of unpopularity.
Nothing is too obscure just now to be blazoned V.R. Even
a benefit concert in a suburban Town Hall “by the friends
and pupils of Mrs. Smith ” is beamed on by both Lord
and Lady Carington, who have absolutely infested
the theatres, and have patronised something or other
every night of the week. Of course this is very nice and
sociable of the distinguished emigrants-expectant, but
the fact that they are emigrants accounts for the milk in
the cocoa-nut, and when the new man arrives we musn’t
expect him to hustle around until his hair catches fire
Always keep your big trump for the last trick. Baron
Carington holds his final lev6e in October.
The long-talked-of Sydney Ladies’ Club has at last
blossomed into life. Its very nice chambers are over the
A. J.S. Bank, in George-street, and the one hundred mem-
bers-the Sydney One Hundred will sound well—are
quite satisfied with the success of their undertaking, so
far. As for the rules, admission can only be gained
through introduction by a member, and as the committee
do not supply name-lists, unless the would-be clubite
catechises her friends individually to discover who
belongs to it and who doesn’t, it is extremely
likely she won’t achieve membership at all. And this
is the Club’s great mistake. If they decreed that intro-
duction by socially well-known people is sufficient to get
the name of a would-be candidate put up, the Club would
escape the imputation of cliqueism. Objectionable people
could be kept out just the same if by any chance they got
so far as nomination, as the ballot-ball would settle the
question. As it stands, the Ladles’ Club seems to be an
institution which must enlarge its mental horizon, or
succumb.
I do not desire to cast any aspersion on the sweetness of
the sighs of Southern society, but don’t you really think
Victorian swains should take solemn warning from the
fact that in every number of a Melbourne weekly they
supply eager correspondents with plans and specifications
of “ how to make pickled onions ”?
Yours affectionately,
SAPPHO SMITH.

Mr. J. O. Dodd, of Carcoar, died in Sydney the other
day. He had long suffered from hydatids, but an accurate
diagnosis was made too late. His death is deeply regretted
in a very wide circle of friends.
A most successful charity concert was held at “ Avon-
more,” Randwick, the residence of Mr. and Mrs. John
Walsh, on 12th inst., when a very strong programme was
presented. Kowalski was at his best in the polonaise in
E flat (Chopin), and Mrs. W. M’Donald sang Weber’s
“ Softly Sighs ” like an angel. There was a large audience,
and at the end of the concert Mrs. Walsh, who wore a
handsome gown of black silk and jet with diamond orna-
ments, entertained the performers and her friends to
supper.

Borough Council usually starts by one member
proposing that the meeting do adjourn until
people have sobered up, whereupon everybody
else points out that the condition of the first
speaker is a dishgrashe to the country. Then they
pass the estimates for a new drain, and go outside
to fight.

Compensation and Confiscation.
The opponents of the proposal to compensate from
the revenue publicans, deprived of their licenses
by the Local Option vote, among other less cogent
arguments, assert, with undoubted justice, that it
would be unfair to tax a family at Wilcannia for
the pecuniary advantage of a suddenly-shut-up
Woolloomoloo publican and the prospective moral
benefit of Woolloomoloo residents in general. On
the other side it is shown that it would be mani-
festly unfair to turn out a man whose license —
subject to forfeiture hitherto only by misconduct
—had been viewed by him as a life-long permit,
with contingency of transfer, to sell alcohol on
certain premises, penniless on the world, master
of no craft, burdened by a wife and family
reared in affluence, or, at least, comfort,
and, consequently, ill - fitted to face the
poverty consonant on this sudden withdrawal
of the sources of subsistence. The Bulletin has
no hesitation in declaring its agreement with the
Local-optionists who object to compensation from
the public treasury, and is also in thorough con-
cordance with those who state that the removal of
a household prop, which long usage has made its
owners consider a vested right, without compensa-
tion is indefensible. The irreconcileableness of
these statements is more apparent than real; for
it will be admitted that the drying-up of certain
beer fountains will not assuage many drinkers’
thirst, and, therefore, that the compulsory retire-
ment of certain publicans from business will result
in a large increase of trade and consequent profit
to the remaining licensees. The inference is
almost unrnistakeable. Should not the neighbour-
ing publicans—whose businesses are thus im-
proved in selling-value and whose yearly re-
turns are largely increased —reimburse to the
State the sums paid as compensation to re-
tiring hotel-keepers by means of an increased
license-fee, either periodic or permanent ? To
this there can be no logical objection; foi* if
you divide the trade of five public-houses among
four, it must result not only in a relative but also
in an absolute increase of profits through a lessen-
ing of expenses in the saving of one house’s rent,
its license, its rates, its wages, its family support,
and wear and tear generally. It is impossible to
please everyone, but this plan—for which the
Bulletin claims originality and an equitable sim-
plicity that may hitherto have prevented its pro-
posal— seems to combine a consideration for the
right of the individual and the claims of the
community that should recommend it to
both liquor and anti-liquor parties. We fail to
see how the temperance party can have any objec-
tion to the tagging of such a stipulation on to
the Licensing Act, and if the publicans want
further arguments in its favour, we need only
point out how gladly traders in any walk of
life would increase their monopoly by buying out
rivals on a system of payments spread out over a
number of years. In the interest of the poor, so
far, neglected, grog-consumer, someone may novr
interject that the landlord will pass the cost of
increased license on to his That may
be ; if so, we trust they Will be benefited by im-
proved accommodation and the obtainal of pure,
State-guaranteed liquor.

Some little time ago an eminent Victorian
barrister was brought to Sydney to plead in a
heavy Riverina case, his brief being marked 500
guineas. A N.S.W. counsel, also engaged in the
case, refused to join him unless his brief was simi-
larly marked. As the attorneys could not do this
the Melbourne man pocketed Ids fee and returned
home.

Sydney Royal has been invariably crowded
from Mr. Toole’s opening night up to date. As stated
last week, the “ Don ” will be the next production,
but, despite Toole’s anxiety to air his “ infinite
variety,” the management, quite satisfied with the
present treasury, wish to defer laying bare J.L.’s
Toolishness as long as possible.
At Her Majesty’s (Sydney) good business
prevails. This is chiefly due to good staging,
especially the realistic rush past of the locomotive
with the night express. Here we are authorised
to state that there is no truth in the rumour that
Hazlitt has accepted the Commissionership of
Railways, and are also reminded that in a Leeds’
production of ‘’After Dark” the engine cut off
the master-carpenter’s fingers. “ Old Tom’s ”
recognition of his daughter is a pathetic and
highly effective piece of acting. Young Greena-
way— not so preachy as in the “Orphans”—
deserves a line for his fine forward fall in
the fourth act, and Harry Jewett his
drooping moustaches and plastered - forward
locks make him resemble G. Y. Brooke —as usual
plays the villain, quietly but effectively. How is
it that one so unvaryingly virtuous in private life
is doomed to such an unending career of villainy
on the boards? With some actors the processes
are reversed. On July sth the venerable “After
Dark” will be phlebotomised, and a transfusion of
fresh blood will also take place, for William Rig-
uold, Roland Watts-Phillips, Bessie Rignold, A.
Greenaway, Madge Seymour, and Frank Ayrton
open at Melbourne Opera House in “ Nowadays,”
and their places will be filled by George Rignold,
Alice Norton, Maribel Greenwood, Hoyt, Hazlitt,
and others.

The substitution of “ Boccaccio ” for
“ Paul Jones,” at Melbourne Opera House, means
a considerable loss of prestige to the Nellie
Stewart season. Saturday night’s performance,
with its frequent periods of solemn silence and
costly abundance of undeserved floral tributes,
partook of the character of a high-class funeral
assisted by a choral service. By this time, doubt-
less, the ceremony is going with a little more dash
and spirit, and melody has vanquished discord,
but under no circumstances can “ Boccaccio,” in-
differently played in several places, avoid un-
favourable comparison with its predecessor. The
story and dialogue passed muster a few years ago,
when nothing better than a combination of
puerility and legs was looked for. There was a
time when the maunderings of those three
musical fellers (whose idiocy finds vent
in playing on their old umbrellas) were
vaguely understood to be funny. They are
insufferable now. A trio of genuine artists like
Snazelle, Gourlay, and Hodson can do no more
than arouse the pity of the many, and the irri-
tating cackle of the few, throughout the first act,
with an occasional laugh later on. What bright-
ness there is in “Boccaccio” falls to the girls,
chorus, and principals, the latter including a
couple of damsels whose lack of excellence is in
strict accordance with the quantity of bouquets
heaped upon them. The enormous success of
“ Paul Jones ” will ensure big business for the
present production, but it will be generally re-
garded as a glittering disappointment all the
same.
# * #
Madame Burton presents a noble spectacle
in the name part (which Nellie Stewart was to
interpret on Tuesday and alternate nights there-
after) and fills the billet with all possible intelli-
gence and sweet music. Snazelle, as Lamber-
tuccio, and John Gourlay, as Lotteringhi, do their
utmost under adversity : likewise Hodson. Wood-
field, too, plays the Prince with humour in his
opening scenes, and Miss Ida Osborne impersonates
the inevitable old woman conscientiously. Other-
wise the company falls yards short of the “Paul
Jones ” standard. Miss Lillie Hamilton (Mrs.
Hamilton Clarke) makes but a tolerable kind of
giddy spouse, and appears to have got there,
instead of the arch Aggie Kelton, by some
mistaken law of selection. Mr. Robert Kennedy’s
pretty little tenor voice is all he has to recommend
him, and on one occasion on Saturday he lost his
redeeming quality by getting horribly out of gear
in a duet with Madame Burton. Again, Miss
Florence Young, a shapely, promising novice with
a good voice, struck the wrong key at a critical
moment, and so spoilt the best efforts of orchestra
and chorus. Miss Ivanova is satisfactory, but
tliiu, and the scenery seems showy rather than
correct. In the attempt to give distance to a
shallow stage, the artist has painted a river clean
out of the level. “Boccaccio” can scarcely help
crowding the Opera House for its three weeks’
run, as per reason above stated.
** # *
“ Sweet Lavender ” loses a little of its
fragrance at the Alexandra, where a popular-
priced audience likes the pathos or fun to be laid
on with a mop rather than with a camel-hair-
brush. A premature stampede from the sixpenny
gallery last Saturday augured badly for the pros-
pects of Pinero’s beautiful comedy, and as the
plot offers no scope for slinging in a breakdown or
a burst of blood and thunder, it may unhappily
have to go before its poetry begins to be under-
stood. Frank Thornton’s version of Dick Phenyl
is still more amusing than comprehensible, and
Lavender shows little improvement except in ap-
pearance. Of other old friends Miss Annie Tay-
lor is good as ever, while Cates has visibly im-
proved his impersonation of the Yankee. Of the
new-comers only Miss Ada Lee is thoroughly ac-
ceptable. Mr. Eille Norwood (who presents a
half-trained appearance) finishes a bad last.
** * *
At the Princess’s recitations by Olive
Berkley and Oracle Hopkins have been addded to
the “ Fauntleroy ” show as that bilious narrative
seemed wanting in weight this trip. A mixing up
of the rival damsels is likewise taking place, and
his little lordship now increases the realism of her
performance by being several sizes bigger in one
act than another. But the stuffing has dropped
out of this dramatic doll, and when Henry Ed-
wards takes his benefit this (Thursday) evening,
they will further introduce a scene from “ Mer-
chant of Venice.” On Saturday next the fair
Mrs. Potter appears with the fair Bellew in
“ David Garrick,” not in “Romeo and Juliet” as
was first announced.
“Joseph’s Sweetheart” is still shaking her
little head to large attendances at Melbourne
Bijou, and Charles Warner is giving his final
gasps at the Royal. To-night sees the last of
“Dora” and “The Barrister,” in both of which
pieces Miss Graci'e seems to follow up her recent
advance movements. To-morrow (Friday) will be
sacred to Mr. C. W.’s benefit and farewell appear-
ance in Australia, a country that has learned to
honour his undoubted ability in the breach
rather than the observance. Now that he is
really about to tear himself off to Drury
Lane, however, there should be something
of a rush to “ Charley’s ” benefit, notwith-
standing that the public have grasped the fact
that very few benefit performances possess any
sentimental claim upon their pecuniary regard.
They all do it, though. Irving and Ellen Terry—
the one making a great income and the other
drawing a colossal salary—take their “ benefits ”
regularly. Toole had a benefit, the other day,
when the management seemed most in need of a
little incidental comfort. And, by the way, our
old friend Herbert Flemming is to have his ben.
and last appearance on Saturday week morning,
also at the Royal. Herbert is fairly entitled to his
turn, after seven years of constant service with the
Firm, and all the talent has been placed at his
disposal.

Dear Bulletin, —Will you allow me to
say a few words about a paragraph which appears
in The Bulletin to the effect that I am said to
have commenced life as a chorus-singer under
William Saurin Lyster’s management at this
theatre? I was born in Englafkl, have never sung
in the chorus, and the first time 1 went on the
stage was as prima donna contralto of the Carl
Rosa Opera Company. My first visit to the colo-
nies was in November last. Apologising for trou-
bling you, and hoping you will contradict the
paragraph, believe me sincerely yours, Marian
Burton. Opera House, Melbourne.

critic ; Sydney audiences have lately had some experiences

Sergeant M’Carthy, late “super” master at Her Majes-
ty’s Theatre, died in the Sydney Infirmary last Sunday
morning from exhaustion supervening a fit with which
he was seized on the preceding Saturday night. The
deceased formerly belonged to the 48th (Foot) Northamp-
tonshire, and was brought to the colonies by Mr. George
Rignold as drill-instructor for the military drama “Youth.”
When the drama was taken to Sandhurst (Vic.) by the
MacMahon firm, Grattan Riggs, Frank Cates, and Lance
Lenton had to appear before “Mac” to be put through
their facings. The trio dressed by their right, Riggs with
his obese proportions, Cates and Lenton with their thread-
paper build. “Mao” ran his eye over them, gave a sigh,
and said in the richest of brogues : “God help the coun-
thry that’s dipinding on yez !” M’Oarthy was invaluable
as a “ super” master, and had filled the post for some
time with Augustus Harris of Drury Lane.

in ’Frisco.

Someone advertises in a Rockhampton paper, apparently
on behalf of Mr. Palmer, who lately performed in Sydney :
“Mr. Arnold sold this right to Mr. Palmer—to use his.
(Mr. Arnold) own words—because as an actor he was
satisfied Mr. Palmer would do justice to the comedy por-
tions of the play; while in the emotional scenes he
expected Mr. Palmer to excel him ; while as a singer no
comparison was desired. Others have tried to pourtray
this character, only to fail ignominiously. They have
neither the voice, presence, or talent required, or the
children, dog, or scenery.”
The Hawk goes for Mesmerist Kennedy. It admits that
his feats are genuine, but suggests that the authorities
should interfere with his exhibition of “ the powers he
possessesover his weak-minded victimsthen it challenges
him to perform his disgusting feats “ on ten out of a hun-
dred persons whom I will provide along with the kerosene,
castor oil, and mustard concoction, and to be armed with
the needle, and to be allowed free access to any patients
which he hypnotises.”

Prof. Douglas Archibald has arrived in Sydney from
America with Edison’s latest thing in phonographs. It
talked off a message from Gladstone to a large assemblage
of theatrical and other personages at a fashionable Sydney
hotel lately. When it had finished, the trouble began—
everybody wanted to communicate alcoholic-tinctured
messages to its little tin ear, which should from its plastic
palate, icons after bear their sonorous accents quivering-
down Jhe grooves of time. At last a well-known Thespian
got a show—bravely and briefly, but with a decidedly
whisky-and-soda intonation, he ejaculated: “I ” (largo
I) “am Fred-e-rick Fitzaltaniont, the original Romeo.”
That filled the machine right up.
According to Harry St. Maur, he has purchased
“Chums,” a New Zealand drama, from David Christie
Murray, “on the biggest terms ever paid to a dramatist.”
There are some good situations in it, but it needs to pass
through the hands of George Rignold.

laide to Sydney Evening News.
A Blackall (Q.) policeman arrested a local amateur actor
for unlawfully wearing a policeman’s uniform. The Bench
dismissed the information and expressed an opinion that
the arresting constable had shown absence of discretion
and wilful disregard of surrounding circumstances and
public liberty.

When Hollingshcad managed London Gaiety, a shop*
walker from a fashionable West End millinery establish*
ment wrote that if he would send them orders for the
play they would send young ladies dressed in toilettes of
the mode, so as greatly to improve the front of the house.
Hollingshead wrote back that they might send the
toilettes, and he would find the ladies to wear them.
At the old Queen’s Theatre, Melbourne, the prompter
got a ben. once a year, in recognition of his taking about
half the parts in the plays. Nowadays, the prompter
seems to gossip with Cerberus at the stage-door. Never
know who is prompter. Have seen a servant-girl doing it,
with a broom in her hand. In a burlesque played all over
England they lost the prompt-book once, and went with-
out an 3'.
J. H. Clyndes has delayed his departure for England in

Company in their Melbourne production of “ Romeo and
Juliet.” Charles Westmacott, a very promising young

Sara Bernhardt lately declared to an interviewer that
she never had any intention of impersonating the Virgin,
though she thought of organising a miracle-play on
Haraucourt’s poem, in which she would have played the
Choeur des Femmes or Mary Magdalen. Further on
she said : “ In any case, I am going away now for 22
months—England, America, and Australia. The further
the better, so far as I am concerned. I have often thought
that I should like to tumble off the edge of the world-
down, down, down, on to some star. Tell me, do you
think they would be astonished to see me ?” Tub Bul-
letin hopes that when Sara does come to Australia she
will not tumble on to any star. Stars always hate one
another like poison.

save for three long straggling hairs. Down came the
curtain with a yell. Bandmann couldn’t find Leopold
after the show. “ Dey call demselves artistes ! Oh, pah !”
