Esoteric Buddhism in Queensland.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
Chapter I.— Chronological.
It was in the year that Benjamin Babbidge
was Mayor of Brisbane. It was also in the year
that Blue-Ribbon Booth conducted a mission in
Brisbane. I remember those two facts well,
because the hero of this over-true anecdote was a
great friend of Ben Babbidge (whose name I also
remember as making rhyme with “cabbage ” in a
Wonderful one-horse localisation of a thre; orse-
and-a dog pantomime run by D’Arcy Stanfield and
Amy Horton); and because, when Booth invited
Babbidge (as Mayor of Brisbane) to preside at one
of his meetings, Benjamin wont to my hero to ask
him to string together for him a suitable speech,
and my hero responded cob amove, for they were
convivial and congenial souls. It is not generally
known that, in tutoring 8.8. for his rather angu-
lar task, Beaton (for such is part of my present
hero’s name; 1 don't give the whole of It as lie is
extant in Brisbane) proposed that the pair should
walk to the scene of the lecture in the Exhibition
Building, at Bowen Park - quite a mile and a-half’s
journey ; nor is it popular knowledge that Beaton
in the between-whiles of inculcating the “points”
to be dwelt upon by the Mayor as chairman of a
teetotal gathering, considered it necessary to call
in, cn route, at every pub. on the road and wash
down Temperance Reform in vinous good wishes ;
but it is matter of well-remembered public history
that Mayor Babbidge did preside over Booth’s
great gathering in a very roseate humour, and
that his speech was a striking departure from the
austere doctrines usually promulgated by bigoted
teetotallers.
Having, by these statements of well-known his-
torical facts, fixed the year, which those interested
can find out for themselves by searching the records
of Mayoralty or Temperance Booms, in Brisbane,
let me, as calmly as I can, proceed to relate
some marvellous psychic occurrences that put into
the shade all the alleged miracles of Madame
Blavatsky and of the
Sacred Brothers of
Thibet. When Oliphant
declared that it was
possible to disintegrate
the soul from the body
and give the liberated
spirit free range over this
planet, he only asserted
half of a psychic common-
place. Bulwer Lytton’s
great Armenian conjurors
who could render them-
selves invisible, and Vic-
tor J, Daley’s pretended
“ Ring of Invisibility ”
(see a late Bulletin),
arc mere fringes of the
mighty possibilities of
psychic science. I know
ray ensuing narration will
be discredited, but I am
content to take my stand
with Copernicus in the
matter of being scoffed
at because I am three
paces ahead of my time.
Let me asseverate, upon
my colonial say-so, that
the following events hap-
pened as I record them.
Chapter 11. Convivial
One Saturday afternoon
I met Beaton, and, as of
mutual consent, weturned
into a hotel together and
“ wined ” —or, to be ac-
curate in this veracious
chronicle, we “ whiski ed. ”
Beaton was (and is) an
extraordinary compound.
Ho was (and is not) em-
ployed in the carpet de-
partment of a Brisbane
wholesale firm. But his
aspirations ran to litera-
ture and plat form ora-
tory, and he was always
bursting over the bounds
of conventionality and
taking to Bohemian ink-
slinging. On this Satur-
day he was going through
one of these Bohemian
crises. He had insisted on celebrating St. Pat-
rick’s Day as a holiday, in spite of the wishes of
“the firm,” and had received his last month’s
salary from that combination. He was especially
caustic in conversation that Saturday afternoon on
the existences ground out by counter-jumpers and
carpet-baggers, and his soul burned for the more
congenial avocation of literature. He revelled in
his dismissal from sordid “graft,” and Bacchana-
lian lights gleamed merrily in his emancipated
eyes.
We talked of occultism and esoteric Buddhism,
of spiritualism and odic forces, we imbibed deeply,
and Beaton laughed to scorn a theory introduced
by myself that it was not only possible for a soul
to escape from its tethers in the body and roam
abroad at will, but that the body and soul together
could be absolutely transmitted in invisible clastic
vapour to any chosen distant part, provided the
apparatus necessary was all on hand.
“ Ha ! the apparatus, of course,” jeered Beaton.
“ So with Archimedes ; he could have moved the
world if he had a lever long enough and a fulcrum
to fit. But he couldn't get the lever. And so you
can crawl out of the bunghole of your theory by
excusing yourself for want of an apparatus.
Talking about bungholes, let’s have another—.”
“ On the contrary, my dear Beaton,” replied I—
“mine’s J.R.D.—the apparatus is all at hand.
Electricity is our forwarding-agent arid we use the
telegraph-wires. ”
*'■ Of course. I have spoken to a man who saw
a man who once knew the man who hung a pair of
boots on the wires, and was satisfied they had
been duly forwarded after a thief had stolen
them.”
“ I came to talk science and not trash,” I re-
plied angrily. “ I tell you seriously that there is
no drawback, provided the subject to be operated
agrees to my holding him body, soul, and vital
principle in chemical solution, forwarding him as
an invisible fluid by electric-wire, electricity itself
being an invisible fluid of a cognate charac-
ter ; re-liquefying him to tire vision, and recon-
structing him, as our school-drilhnaster would say,
‘ as you was afore you was. ’ ”
“ Then, perhaps, you’ll be good enough to trans-
port me out of this accursed town. I should be
transported if you would,” sneered Beaton.
“ Solemnly I will, if you desire it. I can send
you to my brother Tom in Rockhampton, if you
like. He’ll find you a novel telegram, to say the
least of it.”
“ I’m on.”
“ Honor ?”
“Honor bright. I’m on. Lead on. Stay;
have another. ” ;
Chapter IV. — Awful.
We had several “anothers.” Then I led the
way to Professor Pepper’s laboratory, which I
found in charge of young Harry Pepper. In order
to sufficiently arouse Harry’s enthusiasm in the
project I had to order more drinks, and a mystic
cloud hovers over my recollection of the esoteric
rites we observed and the awful ceremonies and
chemical processes through which we labyrinthed.
I remember we chloroformed Beaton and put
him in a vat full of mixed acids.
I remember fainting with horror when a blue
flame, with crimson tongues here and there light-
ing up the violet, permeated the cellar—did I tell
yon that the Professor’s laboratory was in a cellar?
—and a presence seemed to hover all around us, from
all parts of which tinkled multitudinous attempts
at articulate speech.
When I came to, Harry Pepper was grinning
like Mephistopheles through a lurid and purple-
flamed vapour, dense with greenish and snowy
streaks of lamb’s-wool style of smoke-clouds.
“ Where’s Beaton?” I asked.
“There,” pointing to a colorless liquid in the
retort. “ And I’m going to take him to the tele-
graph-office ; the operator on the Rockhampton
line is a friend of mine. We must, however, send
him in one message, or lie'll come out mutilated,
or short of some necessary organ or limb. You
can’t allow waste in concentrated, liquefied mor-
yon know. The contact of the electric
batteries will vaporise him and keep him fast in
one lot, so long as the message is not too much
interrupted. Think out a good, long subject for
a wire to your brother Tom, ”
I could only gasp: “Send him the market
report and commercial news in to-day’s Courier ;
BEFORE MARRIAGE.
AFTER MARRIAGE.
keep on till you use up that ” —pointing to the
retort—and I again relapsed into insensibility. I
suggested the “ market report ” because brother
Tom is in the commercial line of business.
How long I lay 7 in that condition I cannot tell.
My next recollections are of Harry Pepper and an
ass’stant, named Humphries (whom I last saw in
Sydney just before Christmas), rousing me up and
telling me the pubs, were closing for the night,
and that I had tetter pull myself together if I
wanted to carry home some “ stuff ” to see me
through Sunday. (The Brisbane Sunday-closing
law was just then having a spasm of strict ob-
servance.)
I staggered forth and succeeded in joining a
closing rush at the Criterion Hotel, whence I
carried home enough of the vital fluid to “see me
through Sunday.”
Chapter V.-— Missing.
'Mr. Mookelfort, of Charlotte-street, Brisbane,
is a well-known character, and a very prominent
citizen in Protectionist circles. But with ins
social and political ideas, this story has nothin? to
do. He chips in here in his capacity as head of
the house at which Beaton lodged.
It was Monday morning, the Monday morning
after the Saturday afternoon. I was sitting writ-
ing in my little room in Mary-street, and had
banished all thoughts of Beaton, beyond remember-
ing that we had had a remarkably lively time of
it, and wondering whether ho “ had a head on
him ” this morning, and what his views were
about counter jumpers and carpet-baggers, by this
time. A knock concussed at my door, and
Mookelfort ushered himself in. His first question
nearly sent me into a fit. It was—
“ Have you seen anything of Beaton ? ”
“Eh ! No -that is—er —not since Saturday,” I
muttered.
“No? I know all about Saturday. You were
seen drinking with him on Saturday, and he’s
never teen seen since.”
“Didn't he come home?” I feebly asked.
“No. He’s not in the lock-up either. He’s
left his traps untouched. The last seen of him
was in your company. I can’t trace him any
further. I think I’ll go and give information to
the police. Ho may have fallen in the river, or
got cracked on the head and robbed, or—or any-
thing.”
“I wouldn’t give information to the police, if I
were you. You would look so silly when he does
turn up.” (I said the words gaily enough, but my
heart was as sick as a stone-broke penitent.)
“ But Parslow and Hammond wish to take him
on again, and I want to find him to tell him so,”
urged Mockelfort.
“But, you see I responded more gravely—
“ the man may have reasons for hiding, and what
business is it of ours? If he’s in the river, or
cracked on the head, he’ll be found sooner or later,
and we couldn’t bring him to life again, anyhow.
If he is purposely hiding, why should we ‘put
him away’’” *
Mockelfort left only half satisfied, but promised
not to bother further in the matter. But what of
myself? How can I describe the unknown funk
1 was in? What had Harry Pepper and I done
in that awful cellar in those dimly-remembered
orgies which were now one nngraspable phan-
tasmagoria iu my memory? No more work for
me that day! I must go straight away and find
Harry Pepper. _
I found him seated in a dog-cart, just about to
drive off for the day. He carelessly nodded, and
stayed the off-starting horses when I beckoned to
him.
“ Where’s Beaton ?” I asked.
“In Rockhampton, I hope. Goodbye, my
horses are restive and I’m in a hurry.”
ATT Tvwrc Ur,,
Chapter YL—News of Him.
I staggered into a public house and confided my
fears to three tumblers of whisky and lemon,
Then I arose in firmer mood and walked to the
telegraph office.
“Is Mr. in?'’l enquired, mentioning the
name of Harry Pepper’s operator friend; “If so,
tell him Mr. Dipsomaniac would like to see him.”
“ No, sir. But liere’s a telegram for you,” said
the counter-clerk.
I took the missive. It was from Rockhampton.
My heart almost stood still as I read it, then
fluttered furiously, then thumped slowly and
prodigiously, and finally jerked and stopped and
jerked again so spasmodically that I burst into a
loud, maniacal peal of laughter.
It was all true, then. It was not a dream. We
had “occulted ” Beaton to Rockhampton, for here
was my brother Tom’s message announcing his
arrival.
The telegram ran thus :
To Dipsomaniac,— Beatdh has re-precipitated. Some-
thing wrong, however. Crystallisation apparently not
followed proper basic laws. Right leg attached to left-
side socket-front backwards-and vice versa. Sonl
hovering around in blue flame, apparently too large for
reformed body. Wire explicit instructions. {Signed)
Brother Tom.
As soon as I had calmed down my agitation I
sent the following telegram to —
Brother Tom,— Bottle fragments and flame carefully,
await instructions; must read up authorities. ( Signed )
Dipsomaniac.
I dived deeply that night into realms of for-
bidden knowledge, but my sacred vows to the
Brotherhood prevent me taking my readers
generally into my confidence. (Real earnest en-
quirer’s after esoteric truth can be fully informed—
-071 terms.) Next day, I was enabled to send this
telegram to—
Brother Tom,— Re-dissolve Beaton in elecric hath with
fiuoboric, picric and nitro-hydroohloric acids. Cause the
blue flame of his soul to pass in a stream through bi-
sulphide (old bisulphuret) of carbon. Artificially freeze
by ammonia process; subject to a pressure of four
atmospheres. Then simultaneously, by means of re-
agents re-precipitate the body and throw tiro soul in one
vessel, not letting the temperature rise above CO deg.
Fahrenheit, until Beaton is complete. Then follow the
usual esoteric directions for applying warmth and setting
going the vital principle. First food should be strong
beef-tea and old whisky. ( Signed ) Dipsomaniac.
A few hours later this telegram was handed to
me :
To Dipsomaniac,— Beaton revived two hours ago.
Greedily drank whisky ; rejected beef-tea, but managed
some “Irish stew, with Home Rule fixings.” Is now as
tight as a boiled owl. What shall Ido with him. ( Signed )
Brother Tom.
I wired back :
To Brother Tom, —Put Beaton aboard the boat to Bris-
bane. Don’t trust him again by occult despatch.—
(Signed) Dipsomaniac.
I had hardly got home again when this was
placed in my hands :
To Dipsomaniac,—Beaton has vanished. I wash my
hands of the business. No further experiments for me.—
(Signed) Brother Tom.
I let it rest at that. Brother Tom had washed
his hands of Beaton. So would I. He was alive,
anyhow, and safe—that is, as safe as any other
man on a big bender could be. I drank bis health
in solitude and solemn silence.
Chapter VII. —He Turns Up.
Many months had elapsed, and Beaton had
passed out of Brisbane life as though he had never
been, I was sitting in the same room in Mary-
street where Mookelfort had found me one gloomy
afternoon, when I heard the footsteps of a visitor-
on the verandah. I went out and saw a little
man clad in the travel-stained habiliments charac-
teristic of the Queensland bush. He was wrinkled
and thin and yellow, and —he was Beaton !
“I’ve come back from the dead,” he gurgled
sepulchrally ; “come and have a drink with my
corpse. ”
I went. How could I refuse, knowing all I
knew ?
After sundry libations Beaton told me his story.
He had got drunk, he supposed ; and, separating
from me, he further supposed he must have wan-
dered down to the wharf and accidentally gone to
sleep in the Rockhampton steamer. “At any
rate,” he added, “I never came to my senses
until I was in your brother’s house up at Rock-
hampton. Then, thinking the police would be
after me for travelling without a ticket, and also
being pretty hard-up and out of a billet, I bought
a blanket, and started to hoof it in the Central
Districts. I’ve been on the wallaby ever since,
except two spells of typhoid fever I’ve had, and
I’ve got back as hard up as ever. I want you to
introduce a narrative of mine into Bobby Byrne’s
Figaro— ‘A Thousand Miles Humping Biuey’- to
give me a start. What say yon ? ’
Again, what could I say, knowing in my heart
that I was the sole primal cause of all his wander-
ings ? I promised ray
good offices with Bobby,
and Beaton duly made a
modest “ rise ” by his
tale. He has often
spoken since of his
tremendously odd experi-
ence in getting up from
Brisbane to Rockhampton
in a state of oblivion,
and without paying for
the passage. On such
occasions a cloud has
fallen on my spirit, but
I have been dumb and
have never contradicted
him. Nor did I ever ex-
plain to Mock el fort how
Beaton was electrically
transmitted to Rock-
hampton. 1 give the
secret to the world now
for the first time. If
every statement herein
made be not strictly and
literally true, then I give
you leave to call me
The Dipsomaniac.
Berlin Medical Con-
gress, next August, is to
collocate SCOO doctors.
But the doctors won’t tap
the sources of disease any
more than the lawyers
will go in for codifying
the law which they like
to leave a wilderness.
They will just go tickling
and trimming round the
leaves with the pruning-
knife when they ought to
take the axe and lop off
the branches’. With what
sort of a basilisk eye do
the doctors regard a
woman who never was
sick in her life? The
abnormal is their gusto.
The late Emperor’s
throat will be their grand
theme.

The summing-up of Chief Justice Lilley
at Brisbane Supreme Court on 2Gth March last re
Wells, charged witli the murder of J. J. G'Hal-
loi’an at Downfall Creek, affords a singular con-
trast to Judge Mein’s jury-charge in the case of
Jones, of the Eidsvold. Wells caught, or fancied
he caught, O’Halloran m flagrante delicto with his
(W.’s) wife, and killed him. The C.J. argued it
wasacase of justifiable homicide; and, on the jury
returning a verdict of manslaughter with a recom-
mendation to mercy, prisoner was discharged.
Jones, on the other hand, was sent to gaol for life.
We read that amongst the catch-words, or
time-procurers, of the English Parliament the
most marked is Balfour’s “Very well, sir.” This
he repeats constantly by way of marking his divi-
sions, sub-divisions, and the progress of his ora-
torical paragraphs. Sir Charles Russell’s pet
expression is “Let that pass ” ; Gladstone, when
he wants time, takes a drink ; Goschen clears his
throat, and Harcourt drags out a long“ A-ah!”
In the Australian Senates the three favourite
phrases would seem to be —“ Howsumever, as I
wos a-sayin’, an’ I’ll say it agin,” “Yeraliar!”
and “ Come outside an’ do it!”

A Southern county court judge last week
told a man, who when in Sydney was known as
“the champion liar,” and apparently gloried in
the title, that he was “a most unsatisfactory wit-
ness.” It must be gall for a fellow who really
fancies himself as a liar to get such milk-and-water
adulation as this.

century were Zola’s ‘La Terre’ and Walter’s
‘Parnellism and Crime.’ ” and that “the two
meanest apologies on record since the Creation
were Adam’s to God and Attorney-General Web-
ster’s to Parnell.”

him up to a bedpost. Telegrams stopped. Let-
ters stopped. Teeth all extracted.

jokes about undertakers and the dead. John will
yet find that it’s no joke to be dead.

Zealand political circles that Sir Robert Stout has
no intention of re-entering the public arena.

gard the Referendum with suspicion, and the
Spectator styles it “ Mr. Dicey’s Man-trap,”

colonies”

unions! wherefore lie cannot come to Australia.
Is this the man who startled the Clergy Confer-

have become acquainted with that ex-Robespierre,

There is now further evidence that the
conspiracy is nearly ripe to make the conical-
headed Connaught, British Commander-’n-Chief,
vice Gampridge, retire”. Wolseley may now turn
Rad., go on to Republicanism, enter Parliament,
and pour boiling water on the teeming ant-hill of
corruption, nepotism and sinecurism.
Trades-unionist Spence denied the other
day that increased wages caused an increase in the
cost of production, and instanced the fact that
highly paid white labour does more work than
poorly paid black labour. True ; but that does not
affect the point at issue, for if you increase the
wages or shorten the hours of the white man you
will increase the cost of production, though pro-
bably not in direct proportion to the alteration.
Again we say, parting with noble Henry
George, that Robespierre wouldn’t have been
worth a cent as a throne-destroyer if he had been
billed in streamers all over Paris, like Johnny
Toole or Charley Warner. Fancy: “Maximilian
Robespierre to-night at the Salle Ventadour!
Great lecture. Be in time. Only three francs.
Carriages ordered for 10 o’clock.” After that
there would have been no tumbrils for the gu !

The Case of Mrs. Moeller.
GREAT EXCITEMENT IN SYDNEY.
In these days of talking dolls, telegraphs, ocean
cables, telephones, microphones and other electric
inventions, when the occurrences in one part of
the world are telegraphed to the farthest point
therefrom in one day, and when a traveller makes
a journey right round the globe in (!3 days, it
takes something out of the common to startle a
community, but the late experiences of Mrs. Lanre
'Moeller are enough to startle the dead. Mrs.
Moeller, who resides at Newtown, N.S.W., is a
nativeof Germany and came to this country some
years ago. She has suffered from Liver and
Kidney complaints for years, about three months
previous 'to the present time was completely
prostrated and -unable to work; of the
ankles and lower limbs having set in. We diag-
nosed the case ourselves and can truthfully assert
that it was a case of advanced dropsy due to
relaxation and enfeeblement of the membranes of
tlie Liver and Kidneys, which permitted the waste
fluids to exude through them into the muscular
ti sue. We advised the use of Clement’s Tonic
and it was taken for some weeks and has resulted
in a complete cure. Mrs. Moeller herself thus
describes her case :—“Stanley Laundry, Georgina-
street, Newtown, N.S.W. I can with confidence
bear witness to the wonderful efficacy of Clement’s
Tonic in curing Liver and Kidney disease. I
have long been a victim to indigestion (caused by
liver complaint) and an affection of the kidneys.
I suffered terribly from headaches, pains in the
back and side, with obstinate constipation. No
one except myself knows what my sufferings have
been. I was always tired and languid, with no
ability or inclination to perform my daily work.
My tongue was furred in the morning, with a sour
taste in the mouth, and frequent vomiting; one
day I would be pretty well and the next very
distressed or completely prostrated with a very
confirmed languor. This went on until I was so
ill I was completely done up, and quite unable to
move. My ankles being swollen as thick as my
thighs, I consulted physicians all of whom pro-
nounced the case one of Dropsy, they variously
ascribed the cause to be heart, liver or Bright’s
disease. The kidneys were undoubtedly diseased,
because the swollen ankles when pressed with the
finger would leave a dent in the place for days,
and it was easy to press the end of the finger an
inch deep, when the hole would not fill up for
four days. I had heard of the wonderful cures
affected by Clement’s Tonic and commenced its
use. I took three bottles, and the swelling in the
ankles commenced gradually to subside, I con-
tinued the medicine and am now completely cured;
my ankles are their usual size, I have no aches nor
pains, and can do a good day ’s work with pleasure.
My tired, aching feelings have all disappeared,
and I am confident I have been rescued from an
untimely grave by the wonderful virtues of
Clement’s Tonic. My address is at the head of
this letter, and I will reply to any communications
if a stamped envelope is sent with inquiry, as I
consider it my duty to publish a record of my
wonderful cure.—Yours very gratefully, Laure
Mleller.”
Mrs. Moeller’s case is only a prototype of many
others in this country. She obtained a cure by
persistent treatment,' and this is the only way
chronic disease can be cured. A half-hearted
treatment is never satisfactory. You must con-
tinue the remedy for a considerable time if you
want to cure long standing cases. The tendency
after ascertain age is always towards decay, there-
fore if debility has been eating its way into the
system for months or years, it is very unreason-
able to expect one or two bottles to restore perfect
health. All bad cases require protracted treat-
ment.

Society
The Bulletin sincerely hopes that Premier
Parkes’s leg, on which ho has travelled extensh ely
for some weeks past, will soon get thoroughly well.
For one reason, people are sick of seeing it. stated
in print that Governor and Lady Oarington “send
him little delicacies every day,” and that some
self-constituted magnate or other “ writes as fol-
lows.” Who on earth supplies such information
to a grovelling press? The Bulletin is ready to
swear that Governor Oarington, if he does send
the old man a case of fizz, or a frozen peasant, or a
plate of tripe, doesn’t talk about it. The fact of
the matter is that Parkes's leg will be as good as
7000 votes for his party at the general election.
* # #
Says the Hawk (London) :
Lord Bertie, sou and heir of the Earl of Lindsey, whose
marriage was described by my foreign correspondent a
short time ago, returned with his Sydney bride-last week.
And not long ago aJ. R. was struck off the N.S.W.
Commission of the Peace for alluding to England
as a “foreign” country- in which light the
British Tory organs evidently regard- Australia.
Here, again ;
In 1858 Lord Norraanby was appointed Governor of
Nova Scotia, subsequently becoming successively Governor
of Queensland, New Zealand, and finally of Victoria,
N.S.W., which latter position he quitted in 1834, to the
general and universally-expressed regret of all classes of
the community.
And yet Victoria is not situated in N.S.W. at
all. On the contrary, as every intelligent Eng-
lishmen knows, it is situated at Hobart, near
Melbourne, the capital of North Queensland.
* * *
Who would be a N.S.W. Industrial-school
girl? Branded in several places with a hot iron,
kicked on the groin, bashed over the ears and
transferred from one mistress to another without
the knowledge of the ble-sed Boarding-out De-
partment, which is supposed to keep a vigilant
eye on its proteges ! And what luck the branding
lady has ! When brought before the Parramatta
bench the honorary beaks, a set of localists, re-
fused to allow the Stipendiary magistrate to com-
mit her for trial, as was his clear duty, and insisted
that she be let off with a small fine! Another
plea for the abolition of the utterly rotten system
of honorary magistracy. The case affords as
gross an instance of the miscarriage of justice as
did the as yet unpunished vitriol-throwing affair.
# * a
Bishop Moorhouse says that cremation is
not undoetrinal, which is a point gained. Now
we learn that the cost of cremation at Woking
(Eng.) is reduced from a minimum of £3 to a mini-
minissirao of 3s. 6d. Now, what is the orthodox
objection to cremation? Simply because it is so
much like hell. We do not write this in levity,
but to put plain things in plain words. As to
the good Bishop’s saving-clause that earth-burial
is “more decent” than cremation, what does it
amount to? Why, big bustles w-o re decent only
last year.

women so strong-minded as to be absolutely in-
different as to whether or not their )x>dies are
dissected after death. The man who has a horror
of dissection will, on the average, also have a
horror of cremation. As time passes the world
grows more materialistic,
# * #
The best that can be said for most wed-
ding-presents is that they are merely useless, but
there are times when they cause more misery than
did the gorgeously embroidered night-gown pre-
sented to Hercules. James Bullock, a simple
boundary-rider, of Salisbury Plains, N.S.W., was
presented with 2000 shares in the Atlas Engineer-
ing Co., of Sydney, as a marriage-gift. If these
had been 2000 kegs of dynamite and had exploded
on delivery they would have saved James a heap
of trouble. The company bankrupted, and a call
of Bs. a share was made on the shareholders (who
numbered 23 in all, with individual liabilities
ranging from £BO to £3200), and James, it is now
decreed, has to pay £BOO or go insolvent. The
moral is old, and was first stated many years ago
by the eminent Mr. Joseph Geary : “If ever a
man offers you a horse cheap, ask him ‘ Why ?’ ”
* # #
The recent alarming succession of deaths
under chloroform at the Alfred Hospital, Sydney,
is as yet unexplained. There is still much mystery
as to the sources of danger under chloroform.
Most of the medical profession appear to think that
diseaseof the heart renders a patient unfit to take the
anaesthetic in question but the present writer once
heard that admittedly experienced and skilful
surgeon the late ■ ecil Morgan, whose record as an
operator was excellent, laugh to scorn the heart-
disease theory, and say that unless in cases where
people were so absolutely.debilitated that death
was imminent anyhow ho never had the slightest
hesitation in giving chloroform.
Jack Want, Q.C., says that horse-racing,
once the sport of kings, is becoming the sport of
blackguards. To The Bulletin the terms are
synonymous.

Ex-parson W. H. O. Smeaton, now editing
a paper up North, has erupted into verse :
What nonsense ! ’Tis heated my head is
With Chateau Laflcte or Clicquot;
Can it be that the word I have said is
My heart's true confession or no ?
A dance to slow music, in rapture,
A vision in full as my thrall,
I confess to my absolute capture,
O ! Beautiful Bell of the Ball.
From this we reckon that brother Smeaton’s con-
version to the flesh and the Devil is at last com-
plete.

Now that the police and detectives have
failed to find Jacob Rick, and the clairvoyant
lady, who inserted herself into a trance and
looked down the trackless paths of the past in
search of him, has only offered a few general ob-
servations on the subject which are of no use to
anybody, we would suggest that a new scheme be
resorted to. Let it be advertised in all the papers
that someone has died and left Jacob a fortune of
£160,000, and that an additional fortune and a
mansion will be thrown in, provided he marries
the testator’s daughter, the same being a lovely,
blue-eyed maiden of 18, and the chances are that
Mr. Rick will arrive with such velocity that the
public will hear him whiz as he passes through the
air, and a number of people will be overturned
and stamped upon by the legatee in his frantic
rush to collect the money and the girl. At pre-
sent, so far as we see, Mr. Rick has no particular
inducement to show himself, for the instant he
turns up he ceases to be a public character and
drops into his original insignificance ; but there
are quite a number of dead men who would arrive
in great haste for a reasonable compensation, and

# # #
An old woman with a little history died a
few weeks ago at Balclutha, writes a N.Z. corres-
pondent to The Bulletin. She was only working
housekeeper to a poor old German carpenter, but
when she died it was found that she had left £2OOO
to a local storekeeper. The lucky heir knew very
little of his benefactress, and has not the remotest
idea why she left him the money, unless it was
because he always gave her credit when she
required it for herself or her employer. The Ger-
man, who was all along unaware that his house-
keeper was a capitalist, is much aggrieved that
she left him nothing, considering their inti-
mate relations for many years. He feels it all
the more, too, when he remembers a perilous
adventure they once had together. During a big
flood in’7B the cottage in which they lived was
carried down the river for several miles at night
with the pair of them inside of it, roosting as best
they could between the ceiling - and the iron roof.
The old woman was very stout, and when the
cottage threatened to capsize as it sailed along,
the German had to haul her from one side to the
other to keep it level. They were rescued shortly
after daylight, and lived happily together from
that time till the old lady’s death. How she cam-
by her fortune nobody knows.

On the bosom of the Beautiful Harbour
now sail boats bearing hideous pill-advertispments.
The dog-advertisement has not yet reached
Sydney, but is all the rage down South. -For in-
stance, some cigarette people tried it, and now
there are two fine brown dogs going about town
together, each carrying an advertisement - in his
mouth.

Qucenscliffe (Vic.) is remarkable, for the
manner in which its atmosphere makes:-old men
fall in love with young girls. A venerable pastor,
of 75, was there married to sweet 17, or so.
Another of 17, about a year ago, annexed an
octogenarian millionaire geezer, and he planked
down £30,000 for a marriage-settlement. They
went to Europe, where the unfortunate girl died
in childbed, leaving the octogenarian a disconsolate
widower —like Sir Chades Gavan Duffy.

# # *
Two fashionable young bank-clerks, whom
we may as well call Smith and Jones, pondered
ranch on “making a rise,” and agreed that there
was nothing like catching an heiress. Smith met
with an obvious interposition of Providence. He
travelled alone in the Tram-car with an old gentle-
man, who dropped a pocket-book stuffed witli
notes, and Smith on seeing it ran after the owner,
pocket-book in hand. The old gentleman was
profuse in his thanks. “Will you do me the
favour of calling at my house on Thursday evening
—if I’m not there my daughter will receive you. ”
“ Oh !” says Smith to Jones, “ I’m there !” Smith
duly went, and found that the old boy inhabited
a superb mansion. He was let in by a footman,
and received by the daughter ! This was in a
grand drawing-room. She was sorry papa couldn’t
see him, and gave him four half-crowns. He
dashed them at the pier-glass, from whence they
rebounded and smashed an article of vertu, valued
at fifty guineas. For this he was given in charge
to the police! But the old man relented and let
him off.

Theoretically, trust-money paid to a
lawyer on behalf of a client is, unless that client
lias given specific instructions for its investment,
available at call. The lawyer is only in possession
of it to the same extent as an honest bank-clerk is
in possession Of the bank’s cash. But when the
lawyer pockets it, puts it in a mine, or on a horse,
or gives it to a woman to buy sealskin cloaks and
hair-dye,-6r pops it into one of. his own little land-
Swindles, and -then plausibly, lies about it to his
client for two.years, or so,'he doo-n't embezzle it.
Gh no! Once prosecuted, the bank-clerk who
steals a couple of hundred usually goes to gaol
for a few years* even if his friends d.» wish
to make rostitution, but the lawyer who
annexes a-conj of thousand belonging' to the
maiden lady who believes in him because lie wears
a tall hat and is a churchwarden, doesn’t go to
..gaol. He is given time to commit suicide or to go
and scratch" up the money by shearing some of his
other clients. Attorney-General Simp-on has just
submitted a bill amending the N.S. W. criminal
law, but The Bulletin notices it contains no
dames declaring the unauthorised borrowing of
trust-money by lawyers a felony.

* # *
After cutting some jokes over Mrs. Harri-
son’s book on “ Marriage and the Social Evil,” we
may take it seriously on a perusal. We men-
tioned the plain fact that Mrs. Lee is one of the
handsomest and looks one of the healthiest young
ladies in Melbourne, but were not aware then
of another fact disclosed in her work —that she
alleges that her health was broken down by the
marriage system, and restored by the precepts of
Zenobia—laid down in Gibbon.
* * *
The cable’s latest small-beer chronicle is to
the effect that Charles Hern, late of Sydney, has
been requested to send a picture to Marlborough
House for inspection. Which, in plain English,
-means that the Prince of Wales’ daughters’ paint-
ing tutor is graciously permitted to show a little
thing of his own to “ Papa,” who will say, “ Haw!
bewtiful peekeher,” in guttural tones, and so
tighten the silken bonds which bind us, &c. Mr.
Hern’s careful, almost microscopically - worked
pictures—chiefly Blue Mountain scenes —are well-
known on this side of the water.
# * #
The Parliamentary Commission to inquire
into the condition of N.S.W. financial companies
for which Mr. Willis, M.P., is moving, will cause
as big a sensation throughout Australia as the
Premier Permanent Building Society smash. It
will be the means of showing how some irrespon-
sible companies, directed by more or less fraudu-
lent insolvents, get scores of thousands on deposit
from a trusting public by promises of preposterous
interest on the money—which they use to float
schemes of their own. It will show how public
justice is corrupted at its source. It will show
how members of Parliament lend their names to
institutions inconceivably dishonest and corrupt.
The results of the commission ought to be the
sending to gaol for about five years apiece of at
least several people.

According to the Rockhampton papers no
matter who dies his or her death is sure “to cast a
gloom over the town.” If it continues “gloom-
ing” much longer in the city of sin, sweat, and
sorrow, the residents will be able to cut cakes of
blacking out of the darkness.

SCOTT # BOW NE, New York,
infamous practices of barristers. A leading bar-
rister took up his case, on which his all depended,
and conducted it splendidly for a day, but the
next day his place was taken by a strange bar-
rister,' on whom the bulky brief was thrust, be-
cause the other had a case with more money in it.
'J his unprincipled neglect of duty occurs con-
tinually. It is far better to engage a young bar-
rister of genius, who will give his undivided atten-
tion to your case. But then where is your young
barrister of genius ? The most obvious pall iati ve
lies in the amalgamation of the professions.

Sir Arthur Hodgson, once a member of
N.S. W. Assembly, lately spoke at the Colonial In-
stitute concerning a visit to Sydney University
on “ Degree Day —-
It should have been an imposing ceremony, but it was
greatly marred by the undergraduates being permitted to
cany in procession through the, hall and to the rear of
the dais a long pole, with a Guy Fawkes attached, con*
sisting of a very vulgar mask, with a pipe in its mouth,
clothed in a battered cap and gown which would have
done credit to the head and" back of the fastest under-
graduate during my happy days at Cambridge. Lord
Oamnadon made an excellent speech (which was loudly
applauded), unruffled by the vulgarity of Guy Fawkes,
who frequently bowed his assent by the lowering and
hoisting of the pole. Icoukl not understand the conduct
of the Chancellor, in his full academical robes, seated in
his chair of state, and surrounded by numerous colonial
dignitaries, not putting his foot down and suppressing
such tomfoolery.
There now!

* * #
Science is great, and of invention there is
no end. The latest thing is a scheme for applying
otto of roses and other scents, not in the old, mean,
degraded, underhand way by sprinkling the hand-
kerchief or the dress, but by injecting a drop of
the essence into the veins of the arm with the
hypodermic syringe and thus scenting the lady
herself in place of her clothes as heretofore. The
conception is a great one, and the man who struck
it will be remembered by posterity, along with the
giant intellect that first discovered victuals, and
the public benefactor who found out clothes, and
the genius who patented the original method of
coming in out of the wet, and the stupendous soul
who invented sleep—before that blessing was cor-
rupted and undermined by the villainy of the
bloated hypocrite and illiterate apostate who
invented early rising, and the still more stupen-
dous soul who first thought of thinking about
anything at all. And just here an idea strikes us.
When the evening dress gets a very little shorter
than it is now it might be as well to inject it into
the arm along with the scent. It is just possible
that clothes might be a trifle more apparent that
way than when worn in the present superficial
fashion, and, anyhow, they would take root in the
system, and would not present that lamentable
appearance of uncertainty which makes man so
often leave his home and fly into the mountains
for fear that they should prove the forerunners of
worse disaster.

“ Our Boys ” seem to have been slobbered
over at a supper soon after their arrival in London.
The chairman, a party by the name of Dodson,
recited a little thing of his own, from which we
chip one of the least horrible bits :
Yes, England well remembers,
And proudly grasps the hand
Of help you then extended
On Africa’s scorching sand—
It was a graceful action, &c., &c.
While the dunderheaded driveller was thus pour-
ing out his soul on the subject of the Soudan
goat, Sammy Jones dug his companion in the
ribs and said : “ Wouldn't this make The Bulle-
tin proud ? ”

In the absence of the usual truthful yarns
known as “ snake stories,” South Australians are
cultivating mouse yarns. Thus, a country news-
paper relates how a station-hand having killed
3525 mice in three days, a S.A. Register corres-
pondent caps the pile ;
They eat hoots, harness, clothes, and all kinds of garden
stuff; in fact, nothing seems to come amiss to them. I
will now make another attempt to finish this letter, if the
mice will allow me to. I have caught two climbing up
my legs since I sat down. I caught 1437 in one zinc case
this morning, and over 300 in another box. They are so
had in the house that we have had no sleep for two" nights.
I caught three while in bed last night. They bit pieces
out of my toes and fingers and made them bleed. My
fingers are so sore that I can hardly hold my pen to-night.
Next, please !
* * #
Charles Clark is now lecturing in Aus-
tralia. When he last visited Gawler a prominent
citizen named Harris was in his audience and took
the lecturer’s remarks concerning Dickens’ charac-
ter, Mrs. Harris, as being intended for his wife.
The result was that he commenced to harangue
the multitude, but explanations ensued and calm-
ness eventually prevailed. At the same town
Clark also referred in uncomplimentary terms to
“ Prince Charlie,” and a Scotchman present im-
mediately threatened to punch the lecturer's head.
Such a course was prevented, however, by the
police depositing the gentleman on the asphalt on
his head.

Toole is a terror for wanning up his chest-
nuts. At the ‘Natives’” soiree (consisting of
cold turkey and champagne) in Melbourne, the
alleged humorist said: “He always tried to culti-
vate the friendship of the legal gentlemen, because
he never knew what was going to happen (laugh-
ter). If anything should happen to him he hoped
Air. Pur ves would make it three months instead
of six.” Now it happened that at one of those
many farewell banquets in London, whereat the
Lord Chief Justice presided, Toole said that “he
always tried to keep right with Lord Coleridge,
because it might mean getting twelve months
instead of five years”—or words almost exactly
similar. It also occurs to us that we heard
‘Johnny” resuscitate this much-travelled and
footsore funniment, re the advisability of keeping
on good terms with the law, in Sydney Town Hall
at the Mayoral reception twenty minutes after his
arrival from Melbourne. Wherefore we take the
liberty of sounding our little chestnut bell, and
asking J. L. T. to throw farther back for his new
and original jokes.

Dear Bulletin, —I have discovered the
reason of the necessity for Shiels’ Divorce Bill,
the fadure of marriage, and sundry other things.
A friend of mine (female) and a widow now, hail
a most unhappy time of it with the dear departed,
a drunkard who left her with a debt-loaded farm,
a family of 12 children, the youngest a baby in
arms, and never a question as to how she
would live after he was gone. She bravely
faced the music, however, pulled through, and
though seven years’ struggling failed to clear
her entirely of her difficulties, it left her consider-
ably better off, and with a lot to be thankful for.
Marriage was being freely discussed one day in
her presence, and some one, aware of her unhappy
exper.ience,:asked her if she thought it a failure.
Looking her questioner straight in the face in
blankest astonishment, she answered : “ Why,
how can I ? I was only married to one man in my
THE OATH.
They swore—it is a fearful thing—
Those very bad young men,
They swore, and you may bet your boots
That they will swear again.
They swore till husky in the throat,
And as each voice grew thicker,
Each swore a very solemn oath
To have another liquor!
lifetime.” And being married to only one man in
a lifetime is the difficulty we can’t get over if
we don’t succeed in killing our man, and want to
live respectably. It sets us writing essays, and
hankering after Divorce Bills, and wondering
when women will be able to vote, and thinking
we would look well in divided skirts, and the poor
man suffers accordingly. Now, dear Editor,
a paragraph is going round concerning the English
ladies’ craze for men’s saddles, which reads the
wrong way to a colonial. Are you a colonial ?
and if so, were you brought up in the bush ? If
you ’were, you must know that our firmest-seated
riders amongst the bush girls have learnt to keep
their seats on a horse bare-backed, or in a man’s
saddle —but not astride. Perhaps the English-
women are trying to do without the horns of the
saddle ridden on by females nowadays. Give them
the benefit of the doubt, anyway—or are their legs
shorter than the average colonial’s ? lam colonial,
and have ridden astride of a man’s saddle when
skirts were worn a deal fuller than they are now;
I won’t tell you how many years ago, but it was
a question of a two miles’ tramp, or lead a horse
which had never carried a lady. My seat was
none of the surest, so astride it was—and that I
still live is evident by this epistle; but if ever I
catch my daughters at such an indecorous trick
woe betide them. And, apropos of horses, I saw
“Robbery under Arms” —and it (the stage
version I mean) made me so weary. When
I was young—very young—bushrangers after
the stamp of Claude Duval filled my dreams,
but a cuddlesome, comfortable sort of a bush-
ranger like Mr. Dampier makes was never
imagined by me. And Miss Lily has never skipped
round much through the gay Australian bush, or
she’d have a better idea of a bush maiden. Any-
way, one thing I can tell her : when a “ boy ” I
didn’t care about started to kiss me, he generally
did the screaming, or would like to have but for
shame’s sake. There never was any need to call
my brothers by the time I was through with him.
And that brings me to “ bushrangers I have met.”
Only one full-blown and one in embryo. They
were perfectly harmless, too, and had nothing
desperate-looking about them, except the ugly
feet of the elder man; as the old hand who cleaned
the boots remarked, “A fellow wants sixpence
extra for going over the ground on these boots.”
Feet so remarkably studded with bunions I have
never seen outside of a caricature, judging by the
boots. But he was a quiet man, and after staying a
night with us, Swapped horses in peaceable fashion
with our relatives, and departed without any
flourish. His companion was only remarkable for
his sullen demeanour and uncommunicativeness,
most noticeable in a house full of young people,
but he also went on his way, and it was not for a
time afterwards that we young ones knew that
the house had sheltered Power, the bushranger,
and the afterwards infamous Ned Kelly, since be-
lieved to have betrayed Power to the police. My
uncle had known Power as Johnston, a horse-
dealer, some said horse-stealer, back in the
“ fifties,” and I honestly believe he knew him as
Power, the bushranger, also. I can fancy I see
his sardonic grin now, when challenged by our
local trooper with having had that knowledge, and
the twinkle of his eyes when he told the trooper
“it was for him to prove that , and if he had sent
word to the trooper that Power was under his
roof, the trooper would have gone to the hills to
look for that same Power.” The horse he
“dickered ” with Power was advertised, but
found no owner in response, and Power told
my uncle when visited in Pentridge that tho
horse was honestly bought on the Sydney side, if
the money paid for him was not honestly come
by. I remember my sister’s alarm when, in
straightening up the room for the guests, she
found a revolver under the pillow. She was
young, and in her first impulse told uncle of her
find, v.. h a rider adding that she believed the
fellow was no good, and her consternation when
she found the “fellow” at her elbow listening
with a grim smile to her communication. Her
fear was not lessened when she was told she would
be shot if she mentioned the circumstance, but
that threat could not keep her from telling her
sisters. They were the only bushrangers I have
met; nothing Claude Duvalish about either, and
uncle sleeps the sleep of the just I hope. He was
a sarcastic old “ cuss” while living, and enjoyed
a joke thoroughly, so long as it was not at his own
expense Susan. Werribee, April 13th, 1890.

Lansell, the rich Sandhurst reefer, had a
few tons of stone from the 1760-foot level of his
new 180 mine sent to England and made it into
an ornamental rockery about a fountain in his
garden. When he gave up his house he had it
crushed and got as the proceeds £347 Os. sd.
When weight is lost by starving the fat is
almost completely used up, no less than 93 per
cent, being removed ; the heart loses 44, the
muscles in general 42, the bones 17, while the
nervous system loses barely 2 per cent. It is evi-
dent, therefore, that death occurs when the com-
bustible material is completely consumed, and
that every other tissue gives up its components to
save the nervous system.

ioole, they will send to Lassetters where they
may be obtained _ far cheaper, and will give
just as much satisfaction and earn an equal
amount of applause. Lassetters keep the very
best tools which are always good and will last a
lifetime.

(Author of “ Looking Backward.”)

High soul that points them to the perfect way
Of life, when they, unwittingly astray, ’■
Have only hope, most helpless thing can be,
Nor thro’ the darkening portals,can they see,
Of Woe’s drear house of mourning, to the day
When if of dream-truths but one drgam may
stay,
I heir crown of Honour shall be chaste and free.
Praise pales and fails, nor is there offering.
Earth’s great unrestful and unthinking host
Of all who owe thee are thy debtors most;

What Struck Him!
Boston Herald.
“ How are Americans liked in England ? ”
And Air. B. F. Larrabee, of 42 Chester Square,
ex-director of the “New York and Boston De-
spatch Express Company,” who has, recently re-
turned from a considerable residence in London,
answered : “ If they have good recommendations
and behave themselves they are well treated, but
they will like the English people, any way, when
acquaintance ripens into confidence.”
“ How do the English compare with the
Americans ? ”
“ The finest looking men in the world can be
seen on pleasant days of the London sea bn pro-
menading Piccadilly. The English ladies, how-
ever, are neither so neat in appearance nor so
graceful of form and movement as the Americans,
but they seem to enjoy more robust health.”
“Are English-people longer lived than our
people ? ”
“ I don’t know. I have not fully investigated.
But I remember once hearing read a newspaper
paraph entitled, ‘ Why do Englishmen Live
Longer than Americans ?’ That paragraph, by
the way, once solved a great mystery for me.”
“ Ah, indeed, another tribute to the power of
the Press ?” suggested the reporter.
“Yes, if you so please to call it. In 1879, when
I was residing at the Commonwealth Hotel, in
this City, I had occasion to do some business in
Washington-street. When I got to the corner of
Franklin, I seemed to feel a blow in the breast
and fell to the pavement like a dead man. When
I recovered my consciousness I was taken to my
hotel. I first thought perhaps some enemy had
struck me, but my physicians assured me that
such could not be the case, and advised strictest
quiet. For six long weeks I was unable to lie
down. I was violently ill, and my physicians
said I would probably never walk the streets of
Boston again. I did not want to die, but who
can expect to live when all doctors say he cannot?”
And Mr. Larrabee smiled sarcastically, and ex-
pressed himself very freely concerning the number
of common disorders which are controlled by
remedies which physicians will not employ.
“ But how about that paragraph ?”
“Yes, yes. When I was obliged to sit up in
bed day and night for fear of suffocation, and
hourly expected death, my nurse begged the
privilege of reading that paragraph to me. I re-
fused him at first, but he persisted. It described
my condition so exactly, that for the first time I
began to realise what had prostrated me, I was
filled with a strange hope. I at once dismissed my
physician and immediately began Warner’s safe
cure. In a few months I was restored to perfect
health, notwithstanding mine was one of the worst
possible cases of Bright’s disease of the Iddneys,
which all my physicians—and I had the best
specialists in Boston —said was incurable. I tell
you, when a man gets into the desperate condition
I was in, he doesn’t forget what rescues him.”
‘ 1 But were the effects permanent ? ”
“ That was five years ago,” said Mr. Larrabee,
“and for thirty years I have not been so well as
during the past five years. If I had known what
I do now, I would have checked the matter long
ago, for it was in my system for years, revealing
itself in my blood, by frequent attacks of chills,
jaundice, vertigo, typhoid fever, nervousness,
wakeful nights, etc., • etc. I took over forty bottles
before I got up and over one hundred and fifty
before Lwas well. I have commended that treat-
ment in thousands of cases of general debility,
kidney and liver disorder, etc., and have never
heard ill concerning it. I bank on it.”
“Speaking of paragraphs, how do English
papers compare with American in this parti-
cular?”
‘ ‘ Well, they have fewer witty paragraphs, but
the smaller papers, like the Pall Mall Gazette,
St. _ James’ Gazette, and Truth, abound in sharp,
incisive paragraphs without wit. In general,
American papers make the most of news, the
London papers make the most of opinion.”

THE REFEREE.
It is yet premature to hope for ultimate success
on the part of the Australian Eleven. The English
wickets have not yet hardened after the winter, and on
dead ground Turner and Ferris are formidable indeed,
u m ie beginning of the 1888 season one was called a
Terror” and the other a “ Fiend,” but before September
both got knocked about. Then, again, the English bats-
man has yet scarcely “got his eye in,” whereas the
Australians, after playing a whole season, with deck prac-
tice on the voyage and matches at the ports of call, must
be in splendid condition. As the season wears on they
,na y pot stale and the Englishmen must improve.
* * *
i he match with Cambridge University must be
regarded as having knocked a lot of useful glamour off
Australian bowling, especially as it followed directly after
that hard hitting by the M.C.C. at Lord’s. The English
amateurs are sure to slog when the wicket is decent, and
their bowlers aro_ not of much account. And if ‘‘Our
Boys can t punish inferior stuff (which the amateur
article always is) as fast and freely as the other boys can
smite Turner and Ferris, then it is a bad look-out for
them in the long run. We have always maintained that
English professional howling can beat our men on a hat*-
man’s wicket. At Cambridge we had amateur batting
compensating for bad bowling, and knocking up a total
winch was equivalent to 600 from the Australians, if the
difference in attack be considered. The Nottingham
match reads us another rough lesson.
* * *
The life of a professioner cricketer is not so re-
munerative as that of the “cronk” ped, the bullying pirn-,
or the “flash” sculler. In a cricket match there are!
including umpires, about 25 persona to be paid—even
amateurs get their bit—whereas in the other cases only
two parties divide the pool. Then again a cricket match
cannot be easily squared, and is consequently a bad bet-
ting-event. At least five or six men have to‘be “sweet-
ened, and what is known to more than sav two persons
cannot be held a secret.
* * *
Says a London writer:—“A representative
Lngiish team of cricketers would hot be considered com-
plete with the name of W. G. Grace omitted ; but splendid
a performer as this gentleman has been,in his time, it is
more than likely tha,t his actual value is over-estimated
now 7.. There is an etiquette observed in choosin" men to
play in sensational matches. In the first place, there are
the captains of first-class counties to be considered, and
so on, and so forth. This etiquette stands in the way of
ever getting the best men to represent a side, inasmuch
as the captain of a county team is certainly not the
youngest member of an eleven; but if.he elects to play
he probably crowds out a man who, as a captain, is pos-
sibly not so able, but who, as an all-round cricketer is
more serviceable.”
* * #
Dear Bulletin, I cannot concur in your
remarks re the sculling championship. When Ilanlan
came here several years ago, if any sculler had dared to
ass that mighty oarsman to row for £2OO, the Press and a
certain section of the public would have laughed the idea
to scorn. Hanlan was quite right in asking as much as
he liked, and Kemp, in wishing* to continue that practice,
is acting with great foresight, for, as The Bulletin has so
often contended, it should be the policy of every pro-
fessional oarsman to make as much “gilt” as possible ere
he turns into the “sere and yellow” period. First we
have a Canadian dictating how much the championship
prize is to be, and now we have another who wishes to
boss the show. Are we Australians, who have decidedly
shown our superiority in rowing over the entire world
to have no say in the matter at all? I should think
1 , Kemp gives way in this matter, he
should forfeit his own respect and his friends’ <r o od
opinions. O'Connor will yet have to prove he has the ghost
of a show for the championship before he is to be the
almighty Hairy Parkes of the rowing world. Fair play is
the weak spot of Australians, and—O’Connor knows' it
and wishes to work upon them. Of course The Bulletin
will look at it as a “ battle of backers,” but why Kerap’V
backers came in for the brunt, and O’Connor’s syndicate
is sanctified, I know not.—Yours, &c., Australian. '
[You say, why shouldn’t Kemp fix the championship-
stake at £5OO ? Hanlan made it that sum. What we
say is, that the fixing of the stake should not be left in
the hands of Kemp, or any other sculler, gaffer, shandy-
gaffer, or other interested person whatsoever. First
decide what is a fair stake, and then ipake the men
stick to it; otherwise the holder might raise it
to a thousand pounds or two thousand, ° and rhditlv
too, according to your letter. If O’Connor “wins
it, it is quite possible that he will streak hack
home and raise the stakes, in true American bluflin<>- style
against our scullers. What is a fair stake ? (»n reflection
wc think £5OO a fair stake now-a-days, as under the pre-
sent elaborate and expensive system of training it costs
more than £2OO to put a man in his boat fit to row for the
championship, and a man might lose money by winning a
£2OO race. Each man requires at least two boats and
very expensive boats they are. Each must have a
trainer who must also have a couple of boats The
men must be kept like fighting-cocks, the trainer’s
wages paid, hotel bills paid, incidentals paid—
you can take it from us that being “gaffer” to
a sculler is not all beer and skittles. However, this is
beside the point—let the amount of a fair stake be
determined once and for all by a disinterested
tribunal. What we think should be done is, let
the public subscribe for a championship trophy of some
sort, belt or cup or something, and draw up an agree-
ment consisting of one sentence, namely, that the holder
of that trophy is the acknowledged champion of the
world, and that he will row all-comers for it, for stakes
of so much aside; and let every challenger sign this
agreement before he is entitled to a race. If a man wo i
the championship after signing this agreement, then ha
could be made to row for the stake therein specified or
forfeit. At present any champion may raise the stakes to
a thousand or two thousand when so inclined, or mh-ht
quibble and argue and the championship might go“to
America and we never get another race for it. We know
what newspaper fighters are, and the American scullers
appear to be largely newspaper scullers. In fact, just
between ourselves, if a man raked New South Wales with
a tine comb, he might find one or more energetic newspaper
scullers in this country. The plan suggested above would
put things on a better footing.—Ed. B. J
* * *
Sydney made short work of Melbourne in the
Inter-University eight-oar race on the Yana last Satur-
day. Mr. George Upward, one of the best, if not the best
judge in the colonies, said that the Sydney crew were in-
finitely superior to the last Sydney Intercolonial eight.
If such be really the case, the ’Varsity men ought to keep
together and represent N.S.W. in next Intercolonial. On
Saturday the two miles were covered by Si dney in 10 min.
30 secs., and the whole journey in 13 min. 48 secs.
* * * i
Great Scott, how have the mighty fallen!
Bubear, the best man now owned by Great Britain, the
proverbial ruler of the waves, is matched to row the
ex-amateur champion of Victoria.
• * #
Mr. P. J. Clark was the first Sydney amateur
champion in wager-hoats, and as “ Jim ” had compara-
tively nothing to row against, putting a wager-boat race
on a programme was tantamount to handing him over the
“pot." Mr. Gerald Kennedy holds much the same
position now ; lie won the wager-boat race at St. Ignatius
College Regatta as a matter of course on Saturday.
# # *
London Hawk says that sculler O’Connor is too
narrow in the waist ever to he seen in the first class.
That seems to be what is the matter with the average
Ausjralee man, who is Hot built round as a gumtree and
almost flush from under the armpit to the hips, like a
Greek statue. The phenomenal human windmill—like
Spofforth, all legs and \Vings—is well enough in its wav,
but for strength and endurance in heavy work you want
men built like our best footballers and like the English
cricketers and blue-jacke ts.
* # #
The Spencers, although in the interests of sport they
have helped O’Connor, don’t seem to ho over sanguine
about his chances on Monday next in his match with
Stanshury.

otuhvan, at present located in Wellington. He is nego-
tiating with Neil Matterson, and if matters on which they
are m communication can be fixed up, “Sully ” will soon
he on tiie war-path with a challenge. Ho has rowed in
forty races and has only been beaten once.

* * #
Charon won the Hurdle Race at Rand wick on
Saturday. Why didn’t we back him ? Who should be
able to beat Charon getting over the Styx ?
* * *
Yes, it is really wonderful what a week will do
tor some horses. Charon, who ran a bad last., on Saturday
week, last Saturday had no trouble in winning the Hurdle
race.
* * *
The Melbourne Cup, with £lO,OOO added and a
second forfeit of £lO, dosed with 150 entries. Conse-
quently each horse’s present chance is worth about £75
on paper. This is rather different to the Eclipse Stakes,
consisting mainly of subscribers’ money, and in which
each horse s original chance is worth little more than the
cost of entry.
* * *
It seems that Sainfoin, the Derby winner, had
been out once before this season. The colt walked away
Horn a field of horses in the Esher Stakes, at Epsom
Spring meeting, and so impressed the writer of “Sportiw
Notions,” in the Referee, that he said, “ My latest fancy
is for Sainfoin, who may win the Derby.”
* * *
The big horse Tradition was auctioned in Mel-
bourne the other day. Phil. Hey wood, one of the syndi-
cate who parted £3050 for Richmond’s son after his
second to Mentor in the Melbourne Cup, bought back the
burly animal for 900 guineas. Heywood has probably
discovered by' this time that a horse which can nearly' win
a Melbourne Cup when he is beefy, can’t pull off a donkey
race after being made “ thoroughly fit.”
* * *
Jenny, winner of the Great Northern Steeple-
chase, at Auckland, on 7th June, is aflnished jumper and
although on the small side, is very fast. She is by Puriri
out of a Dead Shot mare. .Rick Rae, on old Orangeman’
was never in the hunt, but has a good nag in Donald who
is by Patriarch (N.S.W.) from Flora M‘Donald. ’
* * *
It is perfectly disgusting to see the space the
Australian press devotes to that wealthy but caddish
rowdy “ Abington ” Baird, and yet their’information is
generally incorrect. For George Baird is often confounded
w-uh his cousin, Douglas Baird, owner of Enthusiast and
other good horses.
* * #
Bushman again started first favorite for the
Steeplechase at Randyvick on Saturday, and again the
public cursed. Holman, who last time finished on the
wrong course, fell off on this occasion. What can be the
matter with Mr. Holman’s luck ?
* * *
Englishmen often speak with wonder of our
steeplechasing, and had some of them witnessed the race
just mentioned on Saturday, it would have made them
hold their breath. Native maintained the lead until
about seven furlongs - from home, when Roasiter di*ew
level, and the pair fairly raced at the jumps as if they
were brush hurdles until the 12st. 31b. told on Native
and he fell back. It was yvithout doubt the most des-
perate piece of riding ever seen on Randyvick.
» * #
Rosehill Proprietary Club publish in their race-
books a plan of the course, show'ing the different starting
points. Perhaps Mr. Clibborn may think it yvorth yvhile
to folloyv suit.
* * *
The “ knowing ” people lose no opportunity in
their endeavours to discover if a horse is going “ to jig.”
They yvill follow the owners all o\'er the course trying to
find out if they' are backing their prads, and they will
examine the heels of both horses and jockeys to see if the
former wear shoes and the latter spurs. ’ However the
signs are not infallible. On Saturday, at Randyvick
Muriel’s jockey started spurless for the Winter Stakes but
she got there all the same.
# * - *
The just-defunct Plerroit was so little thought
of by the public that he started at 1000 to 15. He was
beaten badly in the St. Leger, and, barring his Derby win
he never did anything on the turf to command attention’
His progeny, hoyvever, have been big winners in the
classic races.
* * *
Alf. Jacobs, who ought to be a pretty good
judge, admits that the English are, at least, equal to the
Australian jocks, and likely enough the same may be said
of the respective trainers. It is foolish, therefore, to
blame Dawson for Kirkham’s inglorious display. Had the
colt remained here his running would have been just as bad
Then again, this is a good season with the three-year-olds!
The animals ot one season are much in front of those of
the next. For instance, Bend Or, the Derby winner of
1880, was two stone in front of Sir Bevys, the 1879 hero
and Melton, of ’B5, and Merry Hampton, of ’B7, though
fair performers, were just as much behind Ormonde the
1886 winner.
i * # #
M‘Leod, of Newtown, undertook at Foley’s, on
Saturday night, to knock out “ Griffo ” in 15 rounds. ’ He
is some 2st. heavier than “ Griffo,” but the featherweight
champion put M'Leod in his little cot inside tyvo rounds
and Newtown is noyv in mourning.
* * *
Jack Fallon, the Strong Boy of Boston, who did
some exhibition boxing with Peter Jackson in London,
has died of brain-paralysis. Which shews that he pos-
sessed some brain, anyhoyy'.
* * *
The captain of the North Melbourne foot-
ball team, having conducted himself very hilariously
at .a recent match, yvas “reprimanded” by the V.F.A.
President. After a Mr. Evans had expressed his “ deep
sorrow,” “ the President,” in reprimanding Houston, said
“it gave him very much pain to have to do so. . . A
reprimand was the lightest punishment the association
could inflict. . .As a man it was painful to him, but
as president of the association he did not flinch from that
duty.” Brave President ! Standing there, with the eye of
Europe upon him, he dared to reprimand, yes, to repri-
mand an unintellectual young person who had conducted
himself after his own dull, stupid lights. Then the as-
sociation all said they were deeply sorry and went forth
to weep bitterly. There must be something peculiarly
manly about football.

Nfd Chalker died the other day, aged 83.
Chalker was a regular giant, and, in addition to his pugi
hslic abilities, was in his day a first-class long-distance
iuiinei. i His fight with George Huff, who was afterwards
defeated by Black Perry, is the one by which he will be
best remembered in Australian boxiana.
* * *
John L. Sullivan, they say, is again unsober,
but the rumour is probably unfounded. Sullivan has
always treated the reporters with contempt—both here
and abroad they deserve little better as far as pugilism
goes—and they accordingly slate him. This suits the
people behind the big fellow. Of course the public, misled
by the Press, supposed that Kilrain would beat him, and
fell in the soup. In like manner John L. was abused for
not jumping at the, California Club’s terms, but, like Patti
the big fellow stuck out and got his price.
_ W. Hendley, an old-time cricketer of the days
of Wills, Elliott and Marshall, has returned to Melbourne
after an absence of 26 years.
* * *
It is a remarkable fact that though Larry Foley
himself was a noted punisher, his three best pupils in the
several weights divisions—Jackson, Jem Hall and Griffo—
cannot put stingo into their blows. They always lean well
forward as if afraid of body blows, and cannot throw
weight into their deliveries. But as the left is a couple
of seconds faster than the right under ordinary conditions
they anticipate the sloggers who rush in to find them-
selves stopped by blows to which their own attack gives
force. The more swiftly a man fights these, the worse for
himself. The only opponent to meet them is one who
takes things easily and is content to get home an occasional
good blow. This was the secret of Farnan’s success
against Jackson, Hall again—though he won—showed to
poor advantage against Boland, a notoriously slow man
who cannot even use the right, his only good hand.
* # *
Unhonoured and unsung there now lives quietly
in Sydney one of the most successful amateur sprinters
who ever ran on English turf. This is James Douglas
Ogilby, of Sydney Museum, who was amateur champion
of Ireland from 1873 to 1877, and during that period
defeated the best men in England, including Shearman,
MacDougall, Powell, E. J. Davies, &c. Ogilby's brother-
in-law, Reginald Miller, of Dublin University, was in his
day a noted performer at ail distances, and was a short
time ago (he may be still) located in one of the public
offices at Brisbane.

stated.

Sainfoin, the Derby winner, is the joint property
of Robert Porter, his trainer, and Sir R. Jardine, of Castle-
milk, who owned Pretender when he won the Derby.
Sainfoin cost 550 guineas at the ’BB sale of Royal-bred
yearlings, and the owners were offered £6500 and half the
Derby stake if he won after his Esher Stakes victory.

A MODEL YOUNG MAN.
A young man went into one of our city banks a
few days ago. The bank manager, a strict tetotal-
ler, meets him. Several letters are handed in,
which he reads. “ I see by the letters that your
habits are good. Did you ever get drunk?” “No
sir.’ “Did you ever attend any temperance
meeting?” “Yes, sir. The one at the Town
hall the other evening.” “Considerable excite-
ment, wasn’t there? Did you do any good?”
* -No, sir ; I was with the temperance gang.”
Did you ever teach in Sunday School ? ” “ Yes,
sir.” “Did you ever indulge in any of that St.
Louis Lager Beer that has been advertised so
much in the papers ? ” “ Yts, sir.” “ A sensible
man, the billet’s yours ! ”

England.

Little, a Melbourne, undertaker, last ■week-
fell dead while lifting a corpse into ft coffin;
Wolseley and his start’ have received the
clasp for the Suakim campaign, 1885. “ One Who
Was There ” writes to Truth to say :
These gentlemen not only took no part iti the cam-
paign, but arrived after active operations bad ceased {vide
General Graham’s despatches.) They lived on hoard the
Queen, taking occasional trips by sail, and never saw a
Shot fired. As one of those who endured many privations,
Snd saw A good deal of fighting, I protest against this
injustice'.
This is nothing, however, to the injustice shown
to the Australian Contingent. The man who
bought the Suakim donkey had to pay for its
Passage horde, and the man who annexed the
Suakim goat wasn’t even allowed to milk her.

PRIVATE, NERVOUS, AND SKIN
DISEASES.
He has by profound research thoroughly investi-

cated, with these widely recognised qualifications
combined with unswerving faithfulness to his
patients, he has during his practice, extending
over FORTY YEARS, attained a well-deserved
pre-eminence in their treatment. His practice
embraces diseases which probably cause more un-
happiness than any other class of troubles that
afflict mankind.
YOUNG MEN
who suffer from the results of early follies, trans-
gressions or excesses of more nature age, bladder
difficulties, a whitish or dark ropy sediment in

smarting sensation, Headaches, Drowsiness during
