The Coming Fight.
Water versus Whisky.
The Sydney licensed victuallers called a
public meeting in the local Town Hall on Friday
night last, for the purpose of obtaining an expres-
sion of popular opinion on the cold-water party’s
proposal to compulsorily close hotels without com-
pensating their owners. It was the funniest
meeting ever held in Sydney, or any where in the
world we daresay.
* * * *
To begin with, the general public, feeling
that the strong race which has drunk grrgfora
thousand years will continue to drink it for a
thousand years to come, carefully stayed away,
and the body of the hall was simply packed to
suffocation by the temperance bodies, Rechabites,
Good Templars, Sons of Temperance, daughters
of ditto —as many of the cold-water brigade as
could be raked together. On and around the
platform were the licensed victuallers and their
supporters, a sullen and defiant minority. They
saw that their claim had been jumped, that their
own meeting w'as dead against them from the
start, but they had engaged the hall and had
themselves arrived, so they went on with the music.
* * *
There was some difficulty about a chairman.
Nobody seemed anxious for the billet, and a cold-
water humorist in the crowd suggested that they
should “put the billiard-marker in the chair.”
When this trouble was got over, there were no
speakers forthcoming. The chairman stated that
they would be along in a minute, and added that
Mr. Dibbs was to have spoken, but he “ had gone
♦ o Murrurundilater on it was said he had gone to
’ootamundra. Anyhow, he had carefully taken
limself out of the wet. The crowd began to sing
>ut “ Time !” and “ Don’t be afraid !” and after a
vhile Mr, Hunt, licensed victualler, a Crimean
iero by the way, came forward with the first
■esolution, namely, that Local Option without
ompensation was robbery. The publicans, he
said, were a good body of men, theyahvays helped
iveryone that asked them. “Yes, they help
;veryone that has the threepence !” yelled a tem-
oerance fiend. Hunt alleged that they onghtn t
io take away a man's living without compensation,
md he was followed by Mr. F. Bevill, M.A., who
,vas understood to second the motion. The crowd
lidn’t appear to take to him, somehow ; they
started to howl the moment he appeared, and
Kept it up till he sat down. About this time
Gr. H. Reid and “Jack” Want entered the hall,
rmid whoops and cheers. The meeting was
getting more disorderly, and was only carried on
by the courtesy of the temperance organiser, the
remorseless Mr. Clark, who, on promise of being
allowed to move an amendment later on, most
affectively quieted the tumult. How his courtesy
was rewarded by the licensed victuallers will
appear hereafter.
# # *
Concurrently with the arrival of Messrs.
Reid and Want, a person, apparently rather “off
his head,” entered the hall, sat down near the
press table and started to enjoy himself. He was
a fanatic with blazing eyes, half-full of colonial
beer, and lifted by the excitement and the electric
thrill which runs through a crowded and excited
meeting into a state of inspiration border-
ing on frenzy. He acted like the chorus of a
Greek play; he commented on the speakers, he
gave advice, he whooped when the audience
whooped, he cheered on his own account, he was
with difficulty restrained from taking his waist-
coat off when any extra piece of excitement was
going on, and, in fact, he lent to the proceedings
the one touch of completeness which might other-
wise have been wanting. It was a queer scene.
The big hall filled with a clamouring, tumultuous
crowd ; the platform packed with the defiant pub-
licans, who hurled back taunts and gibes at their
opponents ; while near the platform this howling
person raved and mowed and chattered ; and ever
and anon, from the great hall near, came the deep
swelling tones of the organ, “like the sound of a
great Amen,” strong and clear, with its dreamy
music, lifting one’s mind away from the
surging crow'd and their pitiful carnal squabbles.
Mr. Reid said he had only come there to
address both parties, and, in fact, he kept very
cleverly on the rail the whole time. Once he was
interrupted by a temperance man, and made a
rather sharp and stinging retort. The man with
the blazing eyes highly approved. “ Give him a
smack in the snout, George,” he said. “ I’d sooner,
myself, drink gingerbeer than whisky,” said
George Reid. “ Oh, my Gord !” said the crank.
Mr. Reid said that Local Option just now was a
sham —it simply worked for the benefit of the
present holders of licenses. “You want to close
the hotels against the poor man,” said he, “ while
the rich man can drink imported beer. There are
some of you that get sick if you drink beer; this
question does not touch you at all. You say
liquor is bad for a man, but it is only bad when
lie takes too much. Tea is very bad for a man
when he takes too much. Tea ruins your nerves.”
“Tea don’t make a man dance on his wife!”
shrieked the audience. Just here the frenzied one
leaned over and breathed on a man and that man
staggered out of the room with his hand to his
head. Mr. Reid went on and told a couple of old
yarns about children being sent for beer on the sly
on Sunday, but the audience got rather bored and
began to yell out “Time!” and the. orator con-
cluded abruptly; “I can understand your prin-
principles when I see your conduct,” he screamed,
and under cover of this parting shot he retired.
* * #
His place was taken by Mr. “Jack” Want,
Q.C., M.L.A. Jack is a born pugilist, not good
at balancing on a rail. “ I know more about pub-
licans than any man in Sydney,” said he. “ Is it
fair to take aw'ay their living without compensa-
tion? I’d sooner die drinking whisky than live to
drink gingerbeer. Cold water costs you nothing,
and whisky costs me a good deal.” How the tem-
perance brigade whooped ! The lunatic got very
excited, and rose to his feet. “ In about six words
I’ll shut this bloke up,” he said. He was with
difficulty quieted, and then Harry Levien, M.L. A.,
took up the running. The aildience made a good
deal of noise, and he said “ You ve come to ob-
struct freedom of speech, and if you say you
haven’t you’re damned liars”—or words to that
effect. That fetched ’em. They boohooed and
yelled and refused to let him go on till he apolo-
gised, which he did by saying that he had for-
gotten he was not in Parliament. Rough on Par-
liament ! This sort of thing did the publican
party no good, and they settled their own hash by
the tactics which they adopted later on.
# # *
According to the reports of this meeting
in the daily press, a valuable institution which see
saws dreadfully on the liquor question, the temper-
ance party were solely to blame for the
whole concern from first to last, and this,
like most of their reports, conveys an absolutely
wrong impression of what took place. As already
stated, the audience were quietened by Mr.
Clarke, their head ; and Messrs. Crabbe, Perry,
Knapp, &0., who were the leading temperance
people present, actually kept the meeting in hand
and enabled the speeches to be carried on. Of
course, there was a good deal of excitement and
some interruption, but not more than often occurs
at public meetings, Mr. Reid got a very fair
hearing; he ended up abruptly because he never
did like fight, and as oily gammon would not work
and he had the audience dead against him, he
found he was for once not posing as the successful
orator of the meeting, so he went off in a huff.
Mr. Want had a very fair hearing, and his direct
attacks on the Temperance party were evidently
appreciated for their bluntness and courage. Mr.
Levien began by grossly insulting the meeting,
and thoroughly deserved the reception he got in
consequence. Had the publicans been in as large a
majority as the others were, they would have
silenced their opponents altogether. As it was,
by a skilful trick, they made it appear that the
cold-water brigade should move an amendment,
and when “Harry” Levien had finished it was
understood that the Temperance crowd were to be-
gin. They made a move to get on the platform,
but were repulsed by physical force. There was
wild excitement.for a few minutes. A man who
looked like a cheap edition of John the Baptist
tried to get on the platform, and was evidently
heaved off. A bit of a half-hearted rush was made
and some shoving ensued, but no one was hurt.
Then the victuallers put up a man, described as
secretary to a brewery company, apparently to
talk time out. He talked rot and drivel in a kind
of chant, something like the singing bookmaker
who occasionally makes day hideous at suburban
race-meetings. The saints got restive as the farce
went on, but they waited with considerable
patience. “ Let him sing,” said one man, “he is
singing the publicans’ death-song.” At last they
could suffer it no longer. They made another
effort to get on the platform, were shoved off
again, and finally settled on the reporters’ table,
whence Mr. Clarke, mounted on a chair and sur-
rounded by his party, began to address the
audience,
* * *
From this out things were simply.glorious :
the advocates of beer howled wildly on one side
and the cold water set on the other. Not a word
was audible except now and again when in a lull a
fresh charge was made at the platform, but the
publicans kept it, as brave Horatius kept the
gate. Clarke was hoarsely declaiming on the
table : the beer man was shrieking and singing on
the platform—he said something uncomplimentary
about the daughters of temperance once, undone of
them rose up, spread out her ample hand like a
fan, and clawed him down the face—a sample of
the scenes which will occur when women first get
the franchise and proceed to energetically use it.
When you kick a man’s dog, he punches you—
what will happen when a man’s wife is hit in the
course of political discussion ?
# # #
The crank kept ejaculating “Good!”
“ That’s the way*! ” And every now and again
he would throw his head back and swear fluently
and fearfully; as he was sitting between two
of the Daughters of Temperance, it was a bit
rough, and a Temperance young man who sat near
him kept appealing to Mr. George Perry : “ Mr,
Perry, this man’s using ter’ble language ’ere.” As
Perry was about nine seats off he couldn’t do
much, and the Temperance young man seemed
afraid to chuck the loony out except under orders
from thel.O.G.T., but the Daughters of Temper-
ance rose to the occasion, and, after a more than
usually long and profane burst of eloquence, one of
them lifted an umbrella and hit him a whang on
the head that nearly settled him. The chairman
appealed for order, and said that one of their party
had been brutally assaulted ; he referred to the
clawing business, but no one seemed to take any
notice of the crank’s troubles. A wild-eyed
Temperance fanatic made a rush at the platform,
and a licentious victualler caressed him on the
head with a chair, and he retired and sat down
again. Clark was understood to be moving some-
thing or other, full local option without compensa-
tion apparently, and it was carried amid cheers
and counter-cheers, groans, whoops, and cat-calls.
• * *
Two speakers were going all the latter part
of the meeting. The Temperance crowd were on
a table, and every now and again they leaned for-
ward and yelled in the faces of the whiskyites on
the platform, and the whisky brigade yelled back.
Then the Temperance party cheered the Queen on
a cold-water basis, and the opposition cheered her
on a beer footing. Now and again a word or sen-
tence could be caught. “Who is going to com-
pensate us for the loss of our livelihood ?” screamed
the beer advocate. “ Who is going to compensate
the poorhouses and the benevolent asylums for the
folks you’ve driven there?” shouted the enemy.
The riot and uproar were at their height when
Mayor Burdekin appeared, and, speaking firmly
to the crowd, he besought them to go away. Then
some of the Temperance party started to sing, and
before one could realise it they all joined in, and
the whole room rang with the Doxology—
Praise God from Whom all blessings flow.
The beer people looked on in sheer astonishment:
after the tumult and altercation it was a most un-
expected development. They followed it up by
singing “ Auld Lang Syne ” to words of their own,
apparently, and then they cleared out and the
meeting was over'.
# * *
The meeting will, if anything, strengthen
the publicans’ hands, although it looked as if
they put a man up to talk time out and
so baulk the temperance party of their amend-
ment. We don’t like to comment on the
gentleman who took up this task — it is suffi-
cient to say that the temperance folk exhibited a
great deal of patience in listening to such drivel
at all after seeing their leaders chucked off the
platform at a public meeting. The beer party
showed the best generalship throughout; they are
used to little emergencies like chucking men out
of bars, and so forth, while the Temperance people
were a lot of wild-eyed fanatics of very poor phy-
sical development, who lost their heads in the
excitement. They are accustomed to meetings
where nice young men with straggly hair preside,
and the proceedings begin with a hymn and ter-
minate with a collection. This wasn’t that sort of
meeting—not by a whole jugfull.
# * *
And what side is The Bulletin taking ?
That of whisky or that of water ? Neither—or
rather both. It is taking the side of whisky and
water, conditioning only that the whisky shall
be unadulterated, and that every citizen shall be
at liberty to drink whatever he likes.

The society for the Abolition of Capital
Punishment has been reformed in London. Its
head-quarters, pro tern., are the National Liberal
Club.
Tne Bundaberg (Q.) Tribune, another of
the papers which dressed itself in red and thought
to emulate the Bui.i.etin, is dead. Fine feathers
don’t always make fine birds.
Lund’s new line of ocean steamers, be-
tween Melbourne and London, rivalling the
Orient Line, has been started very quietly with
seven new big steamships, built on purpose. The
first leaves Melbourne in the middle of .Line, and
will give Australia another mail.
The principal Mount Morgana ires are said
to have got hold of another good thing—in the
Annie United claim, about twenty miles from
Mount Morgan. The A.E. will, it is alleged, be
worth at least a million. It is being worked by
amalgamation, not by chlorination.
IMMENSE FAT WOMAN.
At Mildura, Victoria, the other day, a woman
was seen to step off the scale weighing 413 pounds,
and she was raised without irrigation. A long,
pinch back skeleton, raising his hat, said “Pardon
me, madam, but where did you get that fat?’
“Not from irrigation, ’ she replied, “but from
drinking that famous St. Louis Lager Beer, the
purest beer in the world.'’
UNIM PEACHABLE TESTIMONU.
Facts are established by testimony. This is so,
not only in laws of business and right, but also in
social life. But while the importance of evidence
is thus everywhere acknowledged, its weight is
purely dependent upon the character of the wit-
nesses. Unimpeachable testimony is therefore what
is wanted. Upon this principle the following
names are presented to the public as being those
of parties who, having experienced or observed
the effects of that great remedy, ST. JACOBS
OIL, accord it their hearty endorsements -

eases. It has benefited me greatly.”

rheumatism and lameness by St. Jacobs oil
was instantaneous.”

ing pain.”

intents named.”

IN SH AKESPEARES DAY
When Shakespeare wrote “Let every man be
master of his' own time, watches were only in-
tended for the nobility ; but, in these days, thanks
to American ingenuity and perfect machinery, a
strong’, accurate, and reliable watch, in the shape
of the Waterbury, has been produced at low cost,
which every cm pi oyer and who under-
stands the importance of having good_ time and the
pleasure and convenience of possessing a correct
and trustworthy timepiece can afford to purchase.
The testimony in favour of the Waterbury is over-
whelming, and, like Tennyson’s brook, is ceaseless,
while its reputation is unrivalled by any other
watch in the world.
Mr. R. G. Spear, 97 King-street, Newtown,
writes: “Having had in my possession one of
your watches for two years X have much pleasure
in stating that it has given me every satisfaction,
and would recommend anyone that required a vc-
liable timekeeper to purchase one.”
Mr. John Robertson, of Minim, near Wallsend,
writes: “I have pleasure in stating that the
Waterbury Watch Company has given me every
satisfaction with their watch, which I have had
for a considerable time, and found it to a
splendid timekeeper throughout, in all climates.
Mr. William Helps, Newtown, Sydney, writes :
“ This is to certify that I have carried a Water-
bury Watch for the last three years, and it has
given me every satisfaction us a timekeeper.
Mr. Thomas Regan, writing from Gundare
Station, Coolah, says;—“l have been wearing a
Waterbury for two years and five months, and it
has kept excellent time . Though I have done some
rough riding and drafting in dusty yards, it kept
good time all through ; and I think they are a gift
to all men, poor and rich alike. ”

Observation by the late Baron Dowse.
“ There are so many men walking up and down
this country crying ‘ God save Ireland,’ that they
are making it very difficult for Him to do it.’
Quite correct. But then, before these men started
walking up and down, Ireland was even more in
want of saving than it is to day, and Providence
didn’t seem to be obtrusively busting itself on
behalf of that bleeding country any more than it
is now.
In the old days the gentleman who is now
Sir George limes, Justice of the N.S. Wales
Supreme Court, was no end of a horseman, and
used to ride his own nag in races over the steeple-
chase course on the flat at the back of where Arn-
rliffe station now stands. His father, Captain
Lines, though he had unfortunately lost one leg,
Was also a good rider. When the first elections
took place he was riding on the Racecourse, as
Hyde Park, Sydney, was then called, when the
crowd hedged him into a corner, and to escape
them he had to put his horse at the high fence
which he took in great style. It was at the same
election that the crowd went to a butcher’s shop
standing at what is now Kidman’s Corner, and
commenced to pull the verandah down. One fel-
low had hold of a post assisting to wrench it out,
when a woman came out with a cleaver and
calmly cut off four of his fingers. Next day an
advertisement appeared, saying that if the per-
son who lost four fingers called at such-and-such
an address they would be returned to him,
A talented American reporter hid himself
in a jury-room, and, while lying doubled up under
the table, took elaborate notes of how the verdict
was arrived at. He is now in the penitentiary for
contempt of court, but his paper had a three-column
article showing bow one juryman didn’t know
which was the plaintiff and which was the defen-
dant, and another thought that probably the
person whose head was found lying on the bank of
a river had cut it off and put it there himself, and
a third wanted to bring in a verdict of aggravated
bigamy in the third degree, until he was overruled
by a more enlightened colleague, who thought the
Poi >e had something to do with it, and maintained
the jury should report that that person was guilty
of poison or other nefarious substance. Also,
there were two men who considered that it pro-
bably arose from spontaneous combustion, and
another who woke up suddenly and urged that
some unspecified individual should be compelled to
marry the girl and contribute to the support of the
infant - only, before he could finish, his next
neighbour stood up excitedly and said that the
contractor ought to be prosecuted for not having
the building properly supported at a time of indus-
trial depression, when the trade was straining
itself to place 5-cent cigars within the reach of the
Great Powers of Europe, and that he believed it
arose through the horse running away, which led
to hydrophobia. Finally all the jury came round
to this view of the case, except one, who asserted
it was a plain case of embezzlement and falsifica-
tion of the books, and a compromise was struck to
the effect that the deceased had been overcome by
the act of (rod and had absconded, and that the
remains belonged to somebody else, but who it
was they, the said jury, couldn’t find out without
an index.
The cheerful and gratifying incident which
created so much excitement at the Melbourne
morgue a few weeks ago seems to be pretty well
forgotten, and we observe with regret that the
only valuable lesson which it was calculated to
teach has been utterly thrown away on a miser-
ably unintelligent public. The great social pro-
blem arising out of the hitherto unsuspected
similarity of females in this country requires to
be met by vigorous action of some kind or it will
lead sooner or later to disastrous results, yet not
one 1 of the ghoul-like journals which habitually
live on human i-emains has even called_ attention
to this view of the case. When the important
public corpse which was destined to produce such
surprising results was taken to the morgue even
the ordinary detective, armed only with his own
naked eye, observed that it had two false teeth
and hair of a particular colour, and he also took
note of its clothes and jewellery ; and when Mr.
Louis Lightman called and shed the silent tear of
sensibility over the departed, he recognised her by
the false teeth and the hair and the clothes and
jewellery. The fact that lie claimed to have im-
ported the remains when they were still endowed
with consciousness and locomotion, and to have
lent money to them, and to have bought jewellery
for them, and furnished a house with a view to
entering into matrimony with them, and the cir-
cumstance that the supposed remains wrote stat-
ing, that the money was only a vision and the
jewel ery a delusion and the furnished house a
de-pa ture from the truth, and the further circum-
staii- e that Mr. Lightman produced 12 letters
written by the alleged corpse, and that the
pair got mixed up in an indignant squabble
like two playful cats endeavouring to digest
each other”s tails in the back yard at
night, are merely side issues. Then someone else
came along and identified the deceased by the hair
and the teeth and the jewellery, and the other
fixings, and it was only when the identified one
came along in person that the weeping relatives
broke up and fled in disorder. Yet immediately
after this failure the stolid plain-clothes officer
went out and proved right off, by the agency of
the same fatal hair and the same insidious teeth,
combined with the clothes and the jewellery as
before, that it was another person altogether, and
by this time it became evident that the Victorian
female was so much alike that it was impossible to
tell her apart unless she wrote to the papers and
said it wasn’t her. The constant repetition of a
female with two false teeth and hair exactly the
same is calculated to demoralise any detective
force, and the unfailing similarity of this female
to herself, together with the resemblance of both
of her to the corpse, and the fact that it after-
wards turns out not to be either her or the corpse,
are circumstances that will ultimately land the
police in a lunatic asylum. Of course if she
habitually asserts that she is not the corpse the
public wants to know who she is, and apparently
she is so much alike that she doesn’t know
herself, for the man she was engaged to
states that she was the female he furnished a
house for, and she says she isn’t which suggests
that he really furnished the house for someone
p'se who was so much like her that he didn’t re-
cognise the difference. Whether the other person
was aware of the mistake, or whether she mistook
him for some other individual altogether at the
time he furnished the house, and whether he lent
the sum of £l5 to either of them, or to a third
party who looked like both of them but was still
quite distinct from the other two, is a question
that is too large to enter into. The one solid fact
amid a wilderness of conjecture is that the female
of the human species is so much alike in Victoria
as to amount to a public danger, and any man who
lends £l5 to her would do well to paint a brief
memorandum of the fact on her in the presence of
a reliable witness, in order to make reasonably
certain that he hasn’t advanced the money to a
complete stranger whom he never saw before.
Also, he had better make dead-sure that he can
identify the reliable witness when he wants him
again, and he would also do well to take all neces-
sary steps to find out who he is himself before he
commits himself, lest he should be lending some
one else’s money to some one else’s girl, and then
when she is found dead, and is suspected of being
1G different individuals aird it is proved that she
is eight of them with an off-chance that she is the
cither eight as well, he may find himself in a serious
difficulty.
The Melbourne G.P.O. Clock-Tower.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
Last of the daughters of Time,
Born to be permanent boast of us,
Over our goodness—and crime,
Smile from your throne on the Post-office :
Say how your Pa’s getting on,
Anxious about him is this city ;
Why are you silent and wan ?
Why should you fear electricity ?
Are you afraid of a shock ?
Coy before sparks and the like of them ?
All that we want is a clock,
Works, and the regular strike of them :
Do you demand the “ Three Eights,”
Rife in this working community ?
Take, then, a hint from your mates—
Strike ! you may strike with impunity.
On the Town Hall is a face,
,Sad, because you are unsisterly,
Yet would it be a disgrace
Were you to pander to Mister Lee?
Grov’llers, dear madam, abound,
Plenteous as rose and chrysanthemum,
Let us hear never a sound
Of the dread “ National Anthem,” m’ra.
We have no yearning for old
Times that are “ loyal ” and “ national;”
So that the time can be told, •
Deuce take the chime variational!
Spread your hands o’er us to bless —
Blessings ! we sadly have need of it —
Smile on the city no less
Though you see nought but the greed of it.
Last of the daughters of Time,
Cause of much mocking and merriment!
Over our swindling and slime
Beam, and have done with “experiment.”
Tell the tale of Time’s flight,
Marking the steps of diurnity,
And through the darkness of night,
Bid us prepare for Eternity.
H.C.C.

will be paid for. Tin Editor will bo (/lad to receive and

otherwise. (Drawings must he made in clean lines
of Indian ink on white paper or Bristol-boanl.)

tvho ought to he celebrated,
d. Humorous poems (from eight to forty-eight lines pre-
ferred).
h. Serious poems, of similar or greater length, especially
those suitable for pictorial illustration.
5. Short Australian or other stories, up to, sag, 3000
words. About half that length, or even less, tvill
be preferred.
All Contributions must be Written Legibly, on One
Side Only of the paper , and folded, not rolled. The
name and address of the writer must appear, in all
cases, on the MS. itself, and not merely on an accompany-
ing letter or paper. The Proprietors do not hold them-
selves responsible for Dejected Contributions; but, if
accompanied by a stamped and addressed envelope or
wrapper, every effort will be made to return them.
The Editor does not undertake to enter into any
correspondence respecting rejected Manuscripts.

Macmorton’s Mare.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
Four years or so ago the death was an-
nounced of a gentleman whose estate was sworn
at a little less than £lOO,OOO, of which, it was
stated, he made a very large proportion by fortu-
nate investments in miningrstook. Not very many
people knew the circumstances under which he
drifted into the wealth-making sphere; the present
writer believes himself to be the only person
acquainted with the story in its strictly correct
form.
* * *
Ten years ago, Macmorton—that wasn’t his
name, but it will do for the present purpose—was
manager of an up-country branch of the Consoli-
dated Bank of Oceana, Limited. The township—
call it Callaba—was situated on the bank of a
river which was sufficiently wide and deep to ren-
der a punt necessary to convey vehicles and horses
to the other side. A few miles distant across the
river was the town (not township, mind, it was far
too important for that designation) of Yowlahmine.
Youlahmine had three banks, and a railway, and
gas, and cabs, all of which luxuries were unknown
at Callaba, and consequently the inhabitants of
the town looked upon the “township” as a very
one-horse sort of a place. But when the extension
of the railway to Callaba was sanctioned and the
building of the line was actually begun, Callaba
began to look quite lively, and the business at the
local bank increased so much that a junior clerk
was appointed to assist the manager. I was that
clerk. Now you will understand how I know so
much about what followed.
# # #
Macmorton, the manager, was frequently
away, interviewing prospective customers, “put-
ting the screw ” on long-winded debtors, and so
on. In his absence I acquired much information
with regard to his private business affairs. For he
was a most careless man, was Macmorton, and
used to leave his correspondence lying around
quite promiscuously, just as if a junior clerk had
no curiosity. Well, it wasn’t long before I dis-
covered one thing about the “boss,” and that was
that he was very hard pushed for money. He had
been speculating largely—always was a great man
for speculation— in land, in mines, and on horse-
races. And he had come to grief, more or less,
over each, but especially over his land-purchases.
You see, he had bought up a lot of land along the
proposed railway-route, intending to have a land-
boom when the line was constructed. But the
route was changed, his allotments became nearly
worthless, and the “boom” did not eventuate.
Thus it came about that Macmorton would have to
find the sum of one thousand pounds to appease
one particularly determined creditor, or declare
himself a “broker.”
# * *
About this time a weekly or fortnightly
visit to Yowlahmine by the Callaba manager was
necessary for exchange purposes. The railway
contractors were customers of the Royal Bank at
Yowlahmine, and paid their men in notes of that
institution. As the works were in progress just
at our doors, a very large proportion of these notes
found their way, in the usual course of business,
into our hands. Indeed, there used to be an
average of £lOOO of Royal notes in our possession
a few days after the fortnightly pay Saturday.
These were taken by one of us to the Yowlahmine
branch of the Royal, and we gob in return their
draft on their head-office for the amount, less any
°f our notes they happened to have. But at this
time we always had a large excess of theirs, on
account of the railway men’s wages. We had no
stated period for keeping the Royal notes, bub
just allowed them to accumulate until there were
enough of them to make it worth while to under-
take the journey. Sometimes Macmorton took
them, sometimes he sent me,
* * #
Well, the time came when we held about
£3O OO worth of Royal notes, and Macmorton pro-
pared to take them to Yowlahmino. I took a note
°f the numbers and amounts. Macmorton packed
them in his despatch-bag, lint it under the seat of
his buggy, and drove off to the punt. How it
happened no one could ever say, for none saw it ;
but when the punt was in the middle of the
stream the ferryman heard a shout and a splash,
and, looking round from the forward part of the
punt, where he was pulling the cable, he saw the
banker’s mare over the side in the water, and a
second later overboard went the buggy, and, as
the ferryman graphically expressed it, the “ whole
fraternity goes to the bottom !”
# # #
i • i i, ... .
feT# 1*
. i ~ ‘ Y c 1S
tressed over the affair, but it struck me very
forcibl on several occasions that Macmorton
wasn> / half so sorry as he tried to make out. Of
. • . , „ .
course theie WciB a Dl2* stu tit our hGciJ ollico over
the j and an insp n ector was senfc up to inquire
bito it. But he could discover nothing more than
was generally known, for the only witness of, the
disaster was Macmorton himself. Then they sent up
a diver, in spite of the assertions of thewaterside au-
thorities of Callaba that the current of their river
was so violent that it was quite ridiculous to suppose
there was the least chance of ever getting back the
bag of notes from its depth—-unless, indeed, it
hadn’t been washed out of the buggy; but that was
very unlikely. The diver spent about a week in
sub-aqueous research, judiciously diversified by
visits to all the pubs., and discovered the buggy
and the dead mare not far from where they went
down, but there was no bag. This confirmed the
pet theory of the residents about the peculiar
ferocity and vigour of the under-current of their
stream, and gratified them accordingly. The diver
was made more of a hero than ever, and was in a
chronic state of “muzziness” without the least
effort or expense on his own part,
# * #
mu nn u , , . , ,
~ be Callaba yokels had never lie fore feasted
le l r e '’ es on 811 9 1 f real live diver, and
their open-mouthed admiration of this amphibious
hero was quite interesting. 1 hey were altogether
too dense, likewise, to notice that the diver s zeal
slackened perceptibly, and his goings-nnder became
much less frequent after a long interview he had
one day with Macmorton in the back parlor of the
pub. But /noticed it. They thought that Mac-
morton was giving instructions as to the best way
to go to work to recover the lost cash, and pro-
bably offering huge bribes to the diver to stimulate
him to carry his mission out successfully. But
anyhow, the result was that the diver went under
water a few more times, studied the interiors of the
various,.pubs, a great many times, and finally
earnedi-the good opinion of the water authorities by
admitting that their ideas were undoubtedly cor
rect. And when at last the toiler of the river an-
nounced that he “guv it up as a bad job,” and
that it was his firm opinion that them there notes
wouldn’t never be seen in this world again, the
folks were tremendously gratified that their pet
theory had been proved correct.
* * *
As for myself, I held my tongue and
kept my eyes open. After expounding a
text from Corinthians to my Sunday - school
class a fortnight after the departure of the
diver, I was inspired to visit the stable-loft during
Mr. Maemorton’s absence, and there, buried deep-
down among the maize in the corn-bin, I dis-
covered the sum of £1837 in Royal Bank notes,
Perhaps the undercurrent had washed them up
there. On his return, just before dinner-time,
that Sabbath evening Mr. Macmorton, I noticed,
also visited the stable. His face at the hotel-
table was as long as a horse’s, and his appetite had
deserted him. He stared hard at me, but I flatter
myself that in my countenance he could see
nothing but the light of true religion.'
A month later, Mr. Macmorton became insol-
vent, and resigned the managership of the bank.
For some reason or other, his bearing towards me
in the latter days of our association was most
chilly and unsympathetic. Be went to Sydney,
became a stock-jobber, allied himself to many
syndicates, and rode successful]y on the crest of
the land boom and afterwards of the silver-boom.
Yet I never envied him, up to the day he died
of typhoid fever, contracted at a Melbourne hotel.
My £1837 has now grown into £ll,OOO under the
stimulative auspices of 60 per cent.

Judging from the latest available reports
~ ■* , • • i i ■ , ,
the bushranging industry is not by any means
what it used to be. Twenty or twenty-five years
ago a bushranger, in anything like a large way of
business, had to hire a trained accountant to
audit the plunder, and it was not at all an unconr
, ru-i-ji .. i
mon circumstance for him to declare two dividends
in the course of a week ; but now all that is
changed, and the small retail assassin of the pre-
sent day is merely a broken-down object of scorn
with a bad cold, caught thr ugh sitting for 18
consecutive hours under a hedge while waiting for
a man who was supposed to have eighteenpence
about him and who never turned up. What ap-
pears to be the la t of the tribe has lately retired
from business in Western Australia, and has
taken to agriculture on a small scale in the hope
that he may ultimately make up his past losses,
He was an active and vigorous freebooter, but on
the first day of his nefarious career he rode 22
mites and only met one man, whom he robbed of
two sixpences, an old knife, and a plug of loath-
some tobacco. He spent that night in a paddock,
and did not dare to light a fire lest justice might
be on his track; also it rained inces-
santly, and he got an attack of rheuma-
tism, and his horse trod on him during the night,
Next morning he managed to raise a breakfast at
the cost of eighteenpence, and late in the after-
noon he was compelled to almost kill a belated
canvasser before he could wrest from him a picture
representing a plain, red-headed girl washing her
feet in a river. He turned the canvasser upside
down and shook him, but nothing fell out, so he
departed with his unsaleable work of art, and for
want of a market ultimately threw it into a water-
hole. About ten o’clock the same night he forced
another lone traveller under pain of death to sur-
render another plug of tobacco, a green apple, and
a foreign nickel coin. His horse went astray
before morning, and the third day he wore his legs
off right up to his stomach trudging along a fearful
road, and met nobody at all, and found nothing
that he could beg or borrow or confiscate ; and he
spent that night repenting under a sodden tree,
while the rain dropped with a maddening persist-
ency on the back of his neck and trickled down
his spine. Towards morning, however, he heard
footsteps coming, along the road, and his spirits
rose, but when he told the traveller to stand and
deliver, that person paid no attention, but walked
right on, and then the freebooter told him that his
gore would be upon his own cranium and fired —at
least, he attempted to fire, but his gun was so
badly soaked that it wouldn’t go off. It took him
six hours to get the weapon in working order
again, and while it was still under repair a swags-
man took advantage of his defenceless condition
and borrowed all his tobacco. Also, sixteen other
people went try in succession, but the instant the
gun was once more in a shooting condition the
procession stopped, and Hot a single individual
who could be robbed of a cent was visible any-
where on the horizon. Long after dark he dis-
covered a selector's shanty in the distance, but by
this time he was too hopelessly broken down to
attempt violence, so he simply crawled into a sort
of barn and went to sleep. By-and-bye he began
to dream, and presently a seraphic vision unrolled
itself before him, and he imagined that, instead of
being a poor, degraded marauder, making war
upon society for one plug of tobacco a
week, he was living in an earthly Paradise, where
he had a billet to drag a cart of dirt uphill
for twelve hours a day at a salary of threepence an
hour, and just as he was asking himself if this
could be real he became aware that a good-sized
animal was trotting restlessly round the barn and
treading on him every time it passed. He sat up
to expostulate, and the animal became scared and
galloped harder, and every time it rushed over
its claws seemed to get hitched in his hair, and it
took a portion of that covering along with it.
Then he clutched at it, but it gave a scream, and
hit out with about 180 weighty extremities at once,
and there Were claws in every one of them ; si the
unhappy pirate concluded that he had tried to
grasp a whole ostrich farm, and lay still lest he
should be eaten alive. Sometimes the bird stopped
and stood on him, but generally it only kicked
him with both feet, and then started off on a fresh
gallop. And so the miserable night passed away,
and at length daylight came and revealed to the
man of blood that he had been insulted for
eight long hours by one solitary Cochin-
China rooster without a tail, and he felt so
badly broken up by this discovery that when the
selector found him and offered him a job to work
seven days in the week in exchange for board and
lodging, he accepted the situation unanimously.
He now trusts by industry and strict attention to
business that he may succeed in retaining this
lucrative appointment all through the winter, and
possibly in the spring he may find an opportunity
to sell his gun for a sum that will keep him in
food while he walks to the nearest town, 150 miles
away, and looks for a situation at a low salary in
a lime-kiln. But, anyhow, he states that he has
permanently retired from the bushranging in-
dustry, the same being a precarious occupation, in
the pursuit of which a man’s constitution is apt to
be undermined through exposure to draughts and
other climatic influences.

The Story of the Big Pearl.
(FOR T HE BULLETIN.)
Chapter I.
Lazily riding at anchor, in company with
some half-dozen others, is a small lugger, one of a
pearling fleet. It is almost a dead calm, and on the
northern horizon there is a hazy suspicion of land,
the coast of New Guinea. The midday spell is
drawing to a close, and the coloured crew are
rousing themselves from their short nap under the
awning stretched amidships. In the cabin, per-
meated, as is all the rest of the Vessel, by an
ancient and fishlike smell, a couple of Malays are
in earnest conference ; Abdrahim, the diver, and
Eyed, his tender. In the bronzed hand of the
former lies a lustrous and beautiful pearl ; a globe
of soft moonlight, such a pearl as is found but
once in two centuries,. Both men know that it is
of priceless value, and are eagerly gloating over
it, discussing, meanwhile, the best means of dis-
posing of their find to the greatest possible advan-
tage. _ Presently Abdrahim wraps up the treasure
in a piece of rag and places it in his box, which he
locks ; then, followed by Eyed, goes on deck attd
proceeds to array himself itt his diving-gear. This
completed, he is helped oveV the side and stands
bn the wooden rung of the short ladder, with his
head and shoulders above the surface of the water
waiting While Eyed and a Kanaka put on the
helmet and screw in the mouthpiece. Take your
last look at the bright sunlight, and inhale your
last breath of the fresh Sea-breeze, A bdrahim, for
that pure; flawless gem has done its work. Eyed
Will not share with you the proceeds of the spoil—
you now go to your death.
The helmeted head disappears beneath the,Un-
ruffled surface of the sea and Eyed takes tils place
at the pump. The nondescript crew pay little
heed to his actions ; again and again the life-line
buivetS; but Eyed is lost in a pleasant dream of
the future in which he wonders at the surpassing
folly of his countryman in trusting his life in his
hands just after showing him the glorious pearl
hidden away in the cabin. Abdrahim, he httoWs
well, is suffocating below the lugger’s keel, but
the Malay’s swarthy features “are calm’ and
emotionless Until he suddenly calls hastily to the
Others, and they drag to the surface the now life-
less form of the diver. An accident, of course-
something must have gone wrong with the pump,
he rapidly explains to the,men as they divest thb
corpse of the dress, and laying it on the deck
cover it with a spare sail. Towards evening a
breeze springs up and the lugger, With the dead
Malay on board, steers for Thursday Island
* * ~ §
The little tbwhship on the island is fast
asleep; bight bells have been struck on board the
and A. steamer at anchor in the stream, and
the last sound of human revelry has died away.
Near an old boatshed in whispered COtivetsatioii
stand Eyed and & Ghitiafflaih The Malay is
excited afid hkger, the Chinaman apathetic, with a
bUrirtlhg assumption of indifference. The big pearl
is being extolled on one side and depreciated on the
other. Subtle Hi Long is assuring Syed that he has
quite over-estimated the worth of his find, and the
Malay is vehemently asserting its wonderful
value. They part, after long dispute, Hi Long
calm and confident, Syed angry and Upset. He
ooils up in his blankets Under the Aid boat-shed,
and after au .hour Of two of troubled tossing falls
iilto a deep slumber that lasts until long after sun-
Hse> When he awakes he sits up, fumbles
Underneath the bundle that serves him for a
pillow, and draws out a common red silk handker-
chief with a knot tied in one corner; glancing
apprehensively around he unties the knot to feast
his gaze upon his treasure; With a yell he Springs
Up, Ills eyes ablaze With fury and despair, for the
big pearl is gone. He has been robbed daring his
late sleep; A mist Of blood swims before his eyes
and blots OUt all his dreams of wealth. Blood—
that is what he wants, and he draws his knife arid
rushes throqgh the door out on to the beach, no
longer a man but a beast of prey, seeking to glut
his raging thirst for vengeance on the first object
crossing his path. A group of men are right in
his, way, and almost before they are aware of it,
he is amongst them striking and stabbing right
and left, seeing before him nothing but a crowd of
grinning Chinese taunting him with the loss of his
fortune. Two are knifed before they can recover
from their surprise—one of the remainder, a
gigantic kanaka, has fortunately an axe in his
hand, and before the madman can stab again
he is cut down. He rises once more with the
blood streaming down his face and rushes out on
the boat-jetty leaving a ruby track on the rough
stones. With one last wild stab at the phantom
fleeing before him, he plunges into the sea and
Syed has gone to reckon with the ghost of the
murdered Abdrahim.
The E. and A. steamer going south that morning
has an additional passenger forwards, in the per-
son of Hi Long, who finds he has urgent business
which compels him to leave Thursday Island im-
mediately.
Chapter 11.
‘ ‘ And what do you think the value of it is ?”
The question was asked by a young man, of an
elderly one, in a small office in Lohdoti.
“ The Value; ttiy dekt boy, is whatever can be
£bfc for it, Getns, as you are aware, can be of such
Valffe as to be almost valueless, because nobody
can be found wealthy enough to afford to purchase
them. The pearl, fabulously supposed to have
been dissolved by Cleopatra, was estimated in
modern money at £BO,OOO. This we may put down
as the romance of history, Cleopatra, a keeh
Woman of the world, had fat to much sense to com-
hllt stioh folly, and £BO,OOO had far more purchas-
ing power then than it has now. However, this is
a most rare and valuable pearl; it is absolutely
flawless, and I may venture to say that it is worth
as much money as you would, make in twenty
years’ work. Now what is the story about it ?”
“ I came home from Singapore, as you know, by
way of Australia. At Thursday Island we took
on a few more Chinese passengers; but before
reaching Cooktown one of these committed suicide
by jumping overboard—it seems he had been gam-
bling and lost all his money. When we got to
Cooktown one of the Chinese came to me and
offered me this pearl for sale. He knew it was
valuable, but had no idea that it was anything ont
of the common. It seems that he won it from the
man who committed suicide, who had got it at
Thursday Island —stolen it, I suppose; anyhow, the
loss of it drove him mad, and he went overboard.
To make a long story short, I gave the fellow fifty
pounds for it, and you are the first who has seen it
since I bought it.”
The elderly man, a well-known and wealthy
dealer in precious stones, looked thoughtful for a
moment. “ Leave it with me for a day or two,
Tom,” he said to his nephew, “and I’ll do the
best I can with it. I know an Indian prince who’s
in London just now, and if I can’t sell it, I may be
able to exchange it for more marketable stones. ”
“To tell you the truth, uncle,” returned Tom.
“ I shall be very glad to handle the cash, so don’t
put too astounding a price on it.”
His uncle smiled as he bade his nephew good
day. “Tom, my boy, don’t you think I’m old
enough to understand my business by this time?
Come to dinner to-morrow and hear how I’ve got
on.”
* * *
The jewel-merchant was sitting in his
office next day ; opposite to him was a swarthy-
faced, well-dressed man of middle age who Was
gazing admiringly at the big pearl.
“I should like to know the story of this pearl
before your nephew bought it,” he said in perfectly
good English.
“In all probability the Chinamah Who Jumped
overboard, bought it for a Song from a diver who
had secreted it. They cannot well sell them
openly, so they have to take what they can get.
Of course, in the first place, it must have been
stolen, but it would be impossible to place the
right owner now.”
“ I am quite convinced that your nephew’s title
to it is good,” replied the prince, “or he would
never have reached home,”
“One of your Eastern fables?” said the merchant
with a slight smile.
“No fable,” returned the Indian ; “ the history
of my country and my own family traditions prove
it. A precious stone gained by fraud or bloodshed
invariably brings ruin on the culprit.”
“That reminds me that on the morning my
nephew left Thursday Island, where the Chinaman
who committed suicide came on board, one of the
Malays engaged in the pearl-fishery ran amok,
seriously wounded two men and jumped into the
sea.”
“In all probability he Was the mail who first
found it; but I WOn’t argue with you, for your
British common-sense is, I well know, not to be
convinced. Will you wait on me to-morrow and
we will come to terms about this piece of a diseased
oyster.”
“I will, prince. When do you intend starting
once more on your travels ?”

“In a few days. lam going right round the
world by Australia. lam on the look-out for a
good secretary ; do you know of one ?” The
merchant shook his head. “I will advertise,”
said his visitor as he left the room.
Chapter 111.
Australia once more. The Indian prince
had purchased the big pearl, had obtained another
treasure in the shape of a perfect secretary, and
was now in Melbourne at the close of the southern
summer. The secretary had been selected out of
many applicants, and, m all respects, had proved
himself fitted for the position.
It is nearly midnight. In a room where evi-
dently a supper-party has just been held are
M'Auliffe, the secretary, and a woman of about
five-and-twenty. She is as faultless in face and
figure as the great pearl itself : but it is a
purely animal beauty. Her eyes have the hard,
fierce light of a bird of prey, but to him they are
as soft as the dove’s.
“ Is your mind made up at last ?” she says.
“It is. Love and fortune with you ;is it likely
I should hesitate.”
“I knew I was right, after all; when that
empty-headed Compton was running you down I
told him what I thought of you.”
“ You did undertake my defence then ? What
did the fool say of me?”
“ That you were only fit to be the servant of a
nigger.”
The young fellow ground his teeth and turned
red and then pale as she repeated the brutal
taunt.
“ I will be even with him before we leave here,”
he muttered.
“Now say good night,” went on Delilah, laying
a firm warm hand on his shoulder. “ To-morrow
we will make our final arrangements to meet in
Brisbane.”
Shortly afterwards M'Auliffe left the place and
hastened to his own home in the house occupied
by the prince. His employer was still up, writing
in a small room that served as an office.
The secretary looked in and the prince beckoned
to him. “Been out to supper?” he asked in a
kindly tone.
M'Auliffe said yes and sat down.
“I am going to have a few friends to dinner
to-morrow,” went on his employer, “ and amongst
them is General Ashdown, who was resident in
the province some years am To-morrow I want,
you to come down with me to the bank and take
charge of some jeWela 1 have there; he is a great
connoisseur, and I especially want to show him the
big pearl.”
After some more conversation on the subject
they separated for the night, and the secretary
went to bad to lie awake thinking of the future.
Never strong-minded, he had been led on to
contemplate robbery, but he never anticipated
auch a ohanoe as awaited him on the morrow. He
had intended to forge the prince’s name for some
£3OOO, and make off with whatever valuables he
could lay hands on, taking with him as a com-
panion the woman he had left that night, No\V the
great pearl that he had heard valued at from forty
to fifty thousand pounds was put in his reach, and
he would indeed be only fit to be “a nigger’s ser-
vant ” if he let slip such a chance.
The dinner-hour had arrived ; M'Auliffe had
been entrusted with the big pearl and a few stones
more rare than intrinsically valuable. He had
pleaded headache as an excuse front being present
at dinner, and the prince now sent a servant to his
room to ask him to bring down the pearl for
inspection.
He was not there, he was not in the house.
The one and truthful conclusion was jumped at
by everybody. He had absconded with the
valuables.
The prince took it with Oriental stoicism
“ Poor fool! ”he said more to himself than to the
company.
“ He’ll never be able to get rid of it,” said
General Ashdown, thinking he referred to the
certainty of detection if he offered the pearl for
sale.
“No,” replied the Indian; “it will get rid of
him, though. That fellow has signed his own
death-warrant. Wait, General, and you will see.”
The escape was well managed, the woman had
planned the details and not a clue was obtained as
to the whereabouts of the thief.
• • • •
A fortnight elapsed when the police received a
telegram from Townsville, Queensland,, stating
that M'Auliffe had been seen and recognised on
the B. and I. steamer Quetta. The prince and
the General were together when the news was
communicated to the former. He shrugged his
shoulders. “I am glad I’m not on board,” he
said to his friend. “ Orders to detain the absconder
were wired to the last port of call at Thursday
Island.”
M'Auliffe and his so-called wife were not
amongst those saved from the wreck of the Quetta.
So with that ill-fated steamer sank the great pearl
and its latest possessors.

THE BULLETIN (Published at Sydney, N.S.W)
The Unique Weekly, Australia’s Nonpareil
Illustrated Newspaper.
The Bulletin, the Premier Australian Literary
Journal, is the only paper which has a con
sistent and distinctive Australian National
Policy,
The Bulletin favours—
A Republican Form of Government
Payment of Members.
One Man, one Vote.
State Revenue derived diiectly from the Land.
Complete Secularisation of State Education,
Reform of the Criminal Code and Prison
System.
A United Australia and Protection against
the World.
The Bulletin denounces- —
Religious Interference in Politics.
Foreign Titles.
The Chinese.
Imperial federation.
The public eye rejects as uninteresting more
than half of what appears in the newspapers of
the day. It is only the other half that is found
in The Bulletin.
Th£ Bulletin is an aggressive Democratic
paper which strives to exclude from its ftrtiatb
cally-condensed columns all matter which is not
of general human interest. The Bulletin was
started seven years ago with no capital but brains,
and has become a vast property, because it pos-
sessed a vitality lacked by the countless news-
paper-ventures in Which Australian capitalists
have iihavailingly spent large fortunes.
The Bulletin’s conductors claim that every
progressive paper on the Australian continent has
more or less endeavoured to profit by its example
and follow its lines. The result of its powerful
influence and its unprecedented literary and com-
mercial success can at once be seen by comparing
the methods pursued by the Australian daily and
Weekly-press prior to £nd immediately after the
first issue of The Bulletin.
From the date of The Bulletin’s inaugural
publication its success was assured. It had at
once a dozen more or less servile imitators, many
of which even took, so far as was possible, its
name, t'llfey are doty all dead, while The Bul-
letin continues the one phenomenal success in
Australian journalism, the one completely non-
local and distinctly universal Australasian news-
paper.
The Bulletin proprietary have, during the
last year, spent some thousands of pounds in
fitting their buildings with the latest appliances
for high-class printing and the production of illus-
trations, and they have now issued The Bulletin
in an entirely new form —printed with new type
of improved legibility on the finest paper, and
adorned with sketches from the hands of the
ablest and best-paid newspaper artists Australia
has ever seen.
The Bulletin is the only well-established Aus-
tralian journal which, throughout its career, has
been consistently Radical. It points with pride
to the fact that for years it stood alone among
secular newspapers in advocating the Irish
National Cause, and that it was the only Aus-
tralian journal which denounced the Soudan
Expedition from its iudeption.
The voice of The Bulletin is the beacon-fire
of national progress : it is the forceful summons to
the crusade against the monopolies in land,
wealth, power, and privilege—the direct cause of
half the misery which now afflicts British
humanity. Against the claims of avaricious
clergy who claim to monopolise salvation, against
the dark despotism of grasping plutocrats, and
against the exercise of any human authority
which has not been granted by the people over
whom it is exercised. The Bulletin will fearlessly
and ruthlessly contend.
The Bulletin, unlike the ordinary weekly
journal, is not loaded with a weight of matter
transferred from the columns of a daily paper
issued from the same office. The Bulletin is
not connected with any other paper, and the
matter it contains is all specially prepared by its
own unequalled staff.
The Bulletin, which is the only paper sold
by runners in the streets of every Australasian
capital, is the most widely-circulated of all papers
published south of the equator, and is the only
Australasian publication which enjoys a popu-
larity unhampered by the political boundaries
that so closely restrict other journals to the
colonies in which they are printed.
The Bulletin’s red cover is equally familiar
to the bushman of the Far North, the stockman
of Central Australia, the pearl-sheller of Torres
Straits, and the digger in the New Zealand
Ranges. A paper which is at once the
most popular city publication and the organ of the
ntelligent bushman must indeed be broadly based.
The circulation of The Bulletin in Sydney is
unrivalled by that of any other weekly journal,
and more than a hundred copies of it are sold in
Melbourne to one of any other paper printed out-
side that city. Perhaps the strongest proof of The
Bulletin’s popularity is found in the unchallenge-
able fact —easily ascertainable from any postal
official—that, of all journals issued in Australasia,
The Bulletin is the one most frequently re-
mailed from one reader to another.
The Bulletin is the journal which, of all Aus-
tralian journals, is most frequently quoted in Eng-
land, America, and the colonies." ...
The Bulletin is going ahead faster than any
other paper, because its vast mass of - original and
specially-written reading matter is the brightest
and best, its illustrations the cleverest and most
artistic, and its policy the boldest and most
straightforward ever submitted to the Australian
public.

I\TEW SOUTH WALES FLOODS RELIEF
IS FUND.
The Central Committee of the New South Wales
Floods Relief Fund, having relieved cases of tem-
porary distress in Sydney, and having now agreed
on the nature of the losses for which compensation
is to be given, are desirous that the subscribed
funds should be distributed as speedily as possible
amongst the sufferers.
With this object in view, the Central Committee
respectfully asks Magistrates or other residents in
all the flooded districts of New South Wales to
have Committees appointed to inquire into the
losses sustained; and where no Committee has
been appointed, the Central Committee requests
Police and other Magistrates to kindly undertake
this duty ; and on their notifying their acceptance
of the position, printed forms for collecting in-
formation as to losses sustained will be imme-
diately forwarded.
In the event of claimants being unable’ (from
illness, distance or any spe ial cause) to present
their claims to the local Committees or Magis-
trates, they may forward such claims direct to the
Central Committee in Sydney.
